Introducing Matter and Its Four Ranks
The author defines matter and lists four ranks, explaining the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of matter as distinct from form, and the four ranks of matter, with a focus on the first rank: matter of craft.

Core Idea
Matter is the shared substance; form gives each object its specific identity.

This passage introduces the foundational concept of matter (al-hayula) in Islamic philosophy and kalam. The author begins with everyday examples—knives, swords, doors, chairs—to illustrate that objects can share the same material but differ in form. He then systematically lists the four ranks of matter, starting with the matter of craft, which is the tangible material that a craftsman works upon.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the examples of iron and wood.
The passage uses concrete examples to clarify abstract concepts.
2
Distinguish matter from form
Identify that matter is the underlying substance shared by objects (e.g., iron), while form is the specific shape or structure that distinguishes them (e.g., knife vs. sword).
3
Memorise the four ranks
The passage states that matter has four ranks: matter of craft, matter of nature, matter of the whole, and first matter. Commit these to memory.
Only the first rank is explained in this passage.
4
Focus on the first rank: matter of craft
Understand the definition: it is every body that a craftsman works upon and imparts a craft into. Review the examples: wood for carpenters, iron for blacksmiths, etc.
This rank is the most tangible and closest to our everyday experience.
5
Reflect on the analogy
Consider how this lower rank sets the stage for the higher ranks of matter. Why might the author start with craft matter?
This prepares you for subsequent lessons on more abstract forms of matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة في معنى الهيولى. نقول: إنا نجد أجساماً مختلفة في الصور، متماثلة في المادة، كالسكين والسيف والفأس والمنشار. فإنها بأسرها معمولة من الحديد، إلا أنها مع اشتراكها في هذا المعنى، يخالف كل واحد منها الآخر في الصورة والشكل. فقلنا: هذه الأشياء هيولاها: الحديد، وصورها مختلفة. وكذلك الباب والسرير والكرسي والسفينة مشتركة في كونها معمولة من الخشب، ومختلفة في الأشكال والصور. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: الهيولى على أربعة مراتب: هيولى الصناعة، وهيولى الطبيعة، وهيولى الكل، وهيولى الأولى. أما المرتبة الأولى: وهي هيولى الصناعة. فهي كل جسم يعمل منه، وفيه للصانع صنعة. كالخشب للنجارين، والحديد للحدادين، والتراب والماء للبنائين، والغزل للحاكة، والدقيق للخبازين. وعلى هذا القياس فكل لا بد له من جسم يعمل منه وفيه: صنعته. فذلك الجسم هو الهيولى [؟]
The introduction on the meaning of matter (al-hayūlā). We say: We find bodies different in forms (ṣuwar), similar in matter (māddah), like the knife, the sword, the axe, and the saw. For all of them are made of iron, but despite their sharing in this meaning, each one differs from the other in form and shape. So we say: these things, their matter is iron, and their forms are different. Likewise, the door, the bed, the chair, and the ship share in being made of wood, and differ in shapes and forms. When you know this, we say: matter is of four ranks: matter of craft (ṣināʿah), matter of nature (ṭabīʿah), matter of the whole (al-kull), and first matter (al-ūlā). As for the first rank: it is matter of craft. It is every body from which something is made, and in which the craftsman has a craft. Like wood for carpenters, iron for blacksmiths, earth and water for builders, yarn for weavers, and flour for bakers. And on this analogy, everything must have a body from which it is made and in which its craft is [made]. So that body is matter [؟].
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. The author begins by giving concrete examples to illustrate the distinction between matter (الهيولى) and form (الصورة). He points out that objects like a knife and a sword are all made of iron (the matter) but differ in shape (the form). Similarly, wooden objects share wood as matter but differ in form. After establishing this concept, he states that matter is of four ranks: (1) matter of craft, (2) matter of nature, (3) matter of the whole, and (4) first matter. He then explains the first rank in detail. Matter of craft is any body that a craftsman works upon and imparts a craft into. He lists examples from various trades: wood for carpenters, iron for blacksmiths, earth and water for builders, yarn for weavers, and flour for bakers. The principle is that every crafted object requires a material body that receives the craft. The passage ends with a statement that such a body is the matter (although the final phrase is incomplete in the text). This introduction sets the stage for discussing higher levels of matter in subsequent sections.

Key Terms
الهيولى
الصورة
المادة
هيولى الصناعة
هيولى الطبيعة
هيولى الكل
هيولى الأولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Matter is only raw physical substance in the modern sense.
Correction: The author includes crafted objects like wood and iron as matter of craft, which is a lower rank of matter, not just raw substance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first rank of matter?
A) Matter of nature
B) Matter of craft
C) First matter
D) Matter of the whole

Key Takeaway
Matter of craft is the tangible substance a craftsman works with, and it is the first of four ranks of matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on the idea that you, like the iron or wood in the passage, are raw matter being shaped by a Craftsman. What form (purpose, character) is being impressed upon you in this stage of your life? Are you actively receiving or resisting that shaping?
?
The passage distinguishes between the substance (matter) and its shape (form). Where in your daily practice of faith are you overly focused on the outward 'form' (rituals, appearance) while neglecting the inner 'substance' (sincerity, intention)? What concrete step will you take to align them?
?
Consider the craftsman's role in the passage: he takes raw matter and gives it form. Who is the ultimate Craftsman over your life? Are you acknowledging His hand in the circumstances that shape you, or do you attribute the shaping solely to people and chance? How can you consciously submit to His crafting today?

The Wax Argument: Magnitude Is Accidental
First proof that magnitude is distinct from bodyness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that magnitude is distinct from bodyness using the example of reshaping wax.

Core Idea
When wax changes shape, its bodyness stays; magnitude changes – so they differ.

The author begins by stating that while body is never free of magnitude, they are metaphysically distinct. The first proof uses a simple physical example: a piece of wax retains its identity as a body while its magnitude changes through shaping. This shows that magnitude is an accident, not essential.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the wax example and the conclusion 'the permanent is distinct from the non-permanent.'
The example assumes that bodyness persists despite shape changes.
2
Identify the key concepts
Note the terms: 'magnitude' (al-miqdar), 'bodyness' (al-jismiyya), and the distinction between permanent and changing.
3
Apply the reasoning
Think of other examples where a substance remains the same but its quantitative attributes change. Why does this prove that magnitude is not part of the essence?
The logic: if x changes while y stays, x ≠ y.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدار والحجمية ، فإن ذات الجسم ، وإن كان لا ينفك عنهما ، لا في الوجود الخارجي، ولا في الوجود الذهني . إلا أنه ثبت بالدليل : أنه مغاير للجسمية . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنا إذا أخذنا قطعة من الشمعة ، وشكلناها بالأشكال المختلفة ، فإن الجسمية الواحدة بعينها باقية ، وأما المقادير المختلفة ، فهي متعاقبة عليها ، والباقي مغاير لما هو غير باق . والثاني : وهو أن الاجسام متساوية في الجسمية ، ومختلفة في المقادير . وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة . فالجسمية مغايرة للمقادير . والثالث : إنه ثبت بالدليل : أن الجسم الواحد مع بقاء ذاته ، يقبل التخلخل والتكاثف. فههنا ذات الجسم الواحد باقية بعينها ، مع المقادير المختلفة متواردة عليها ، فوجب أن يكون المقدار مغايراً لذات الجسم . قالوا : فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن كون الجسم جسماً ، أمر مغاير لكونه طويلاً عريضاً عميقاً ، فامتنع تعريف الجسم بهذا الحد.
As for magnitude and volume, the essence of body, although not free of them either in external existence or in mental existence, yet it has been established by proof that it is distinct from bodyness. Evidence for this: First: If we take a piece of wax and shape it into different forms, the one bodyness remains the same, while the different magnitudes succeed it. The permanent is distinct from the non-permanent. Second: Bodies are equal in bodyness but different in magnitudes. That by which they share is other than that by which they differ. So bodyness is distinct from magnitudes. Third: It has been established by proof that one body, while its essence remains, can undergo rarefaction and condensation. So here the one body's essence remains the same while different magnitudes follow upon it. Therefore magnitude must be distinct from the body's essence. They said: Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that being a body is distinct from being long, wide, deep, so it is impossible to define body by this limit.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a concise presentation of three proofs that magnitude (miqdar) is distinct from bodyness (jismiyya). The author first notes that while body never lacks magnitude, the two are metaphysically distinct. Proof 1: A piece of wax retains its bodyness while its magnitude changes (e.g., reshaped), so the permanent (bodyness) is distinct from the changing (magnitude). Proof 2: All bodies share bodyness but differ in magnitude; that which is common differs from that which distinguishes. Proof 3: Rarefaction and condensation show the same body essence accepting different magnitudes, so magnitude is an accident. The conclusion: being a body is not equivalent to being long, wide, deep, thus the Mu'tazilite definition fails.

Key Terms
المقدار
الحجمية
الجسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since magnitude always accompanies body, it must be essential.
Correction: The wax example shows that a constant (bodyness) can have changing accidents (magnitudes).

Checkpoint
What is the key observation in the wax argument?
A) The wax changes color
B) The bodyness remains while magnitude changes
C) The wax disappears
D) The magnitude stays the same

Key Takeaway
The wax argument proves that magnitude is distinct from bodyness because bodyness can remain while magnitude changes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the wax’s bodyness remains unchanged while its size and shape come and go, what does that say about you? What part of you is truly permanent, and how much of your time do you spend obsessing over things that are constantly changing like your appearance, wealth, or reputation? Write one practical change you will make today to shift focus from the accidental to the essential.
?
The proof concludes that the permanent is distinct from the non-permanent. Consider the permanent aspects of your faith (like tawheed) and the temporary aspects (like worldly successes). Are you prioritizing the permanent or the temporary? What is one thing you can do today to make your eternal essence more real than your fleeting circumstances?
?
The argument uses a simple physical observation to reveal a deep truth about reality. Look at your own life—what is one changeable attribute (like your job, health, or social circle) that you have been treating as if it defines who you are? How would your decisions be different if you truly believed that attribute is not part of your essence?

The Argument from Commonality and Difference
Second proof that magnitude is distinct from bodyness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that magnitude is distinct from bodyness based on the logical principle that what is shared differs from what distinguishes.

Core Idea
All bodies share bodyness but differ in magnitude; therefore they are distinct.

The second proof is a logical argument: all bodies are equal in bodyness but vary in magnitude. Whatever causes the commonality (bodyness) cannot be the same as whatever causes the difference (magnitude). This reinforces the conclusion that magnitude is an accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the terms 'equal in bodyness' and 'different in magnitudes.'
This is a simple logical deduction.
2
Understand the logical principle
If two things are shared by all members of a class, and another two things differentiate them, the shared and differentiating attributes must be different.
Example: all humans share humanity but differ in height. Humanity ≠ height.
3
Apply to bodies
All bodies share bodyness; they differ in magnitude. Hence bodyness ≠ magnitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدار والحجمية ، فإن ذات الجسم ، وإن كان لا ينفك عنهما ، لا في الوجود الخارجي، ولا في الوجود الذهني . إلا أنه ثبت بالدليل : أنه مغاير للجسمية . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنا إذا أخذنا قطعة من الشمعة ، وشكلناها بالأشكال المختلفة ، فإن الجسمية الواحدة بعينها باقية ، وأما المقادير المختلفة ، فهي متعاقبة عليها ، والباقي مغاير لما هو غير باق . والثاني : وهو أن الاجسام متساوية في الجسمية ، ومختلفة في المقادير . وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة . فالجسمية مغايرة للمقادير . والثالث : إنه ثبت بالدليل : أن الجسم الواحد مع بقاء ذاته ، يقبل التخلخل والتكاثف. فههنا ذات الجسم الواحد باقية بعينها ، مع المقادير المختلفة متواردة عليها ، فوجب أن يكون المقدار مغايراً لذات الجسم . قالوا : فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن كون الجسم جسماً ، أمر مغاير لكونه طويلاً عريضاً عميقاً ، فامتنع تعريف الجسم بهذا الحد.
As for magnitude and volume, the essence of body, although not free of them either in external existence or in mental existence, yet it has been established by proof that it is distinct from bodyness. Evidence for this: First: If we take a piece of wax and shape it into different forms, the one bodyness remains the same, while the different magnitudes succeed it. So here the one body's essence remains the same while different magnitudes follow upon it. Therefore magnitude must be distinct from the body's essence. They said: Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that being a body is distinct from being long, wide, deep, so it is impossible to define body by this limit.

The author first notes that while body never lacks magnitude, the two are metaphysically distinct. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson uses commonality and difference to distinguish bodyness from magnitude. Apply the same logical principle to your own faith: you share belief in Allah with other Muslims, but your level of obedience differs. How does this distinction reveal a deeper accountability for your actions, and what will you change today to align your actions with your confession?
?
The argument shows that two attributes can be necessarily present together yet remain distinct. Consider your own character: are there traits you assumed were inseparable (like patience and calmness) that might actually be distinct? How might recognizing this distinction help you grow in virtue?

The Argument from Rarefaction and Condensation
Third proof and final conclusion that magnitude is distinct from bodyness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that magnitude is distinct from bodyness using the phenomena of rarefaction and condensation.

Core Idea
A body can expand or contract while remaining the same; so magnitude is accidental.

The third proof appeals to physical processes: a body can undergo rarefaction (expansion) or condensation (contraction) while retaining its identity. The same essence persists despite different magnitudes, proving that magnitude is not essential to bodyness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the terms 'rarefaction' (takhallul) and 'condensation' (takathuf).
These are well-known physical changes.
2
Understand the reasoning
If the essence stays the same while magnitude varies, magnitude must be an accident, not part of the essence.
3
Connect to the conclusion
The author concludes that being a body is not the same as being long, wide, deep — refuting the Mu'tazilite definition.
This refutes the definition of body as extended substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدار والحجمية ، فإن ذات الجسم ، وإن كان لا ينفك عنهما ، لا في الوجود الخارجي، ولا في الوجود الذهني . إلا أنه ثبت بالدليل : أنه مغاير للجسمية . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنا إذا أخذنا قطعة من الشمعة ، وشكلناها بالأشكال المختلفة ، فإن الجسمية الواحدة بعينها باقية ، وأما المقادير المختلفة ، فهي متعاقبة عليها ، والباقي مغاير لما هو غير باق . والثاني : وهو أن الاجسام متساوية في الجسمية ، ومختلفة في المقادير . وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة . فالجسمية مغايرة للمقادير . والثالث : إنه ثبت بالدليل : أن الجسم الواحد مع بقاء ذاته ، يقبل التخلخل والتكاثف. فههنا ذات الجسم الواحد باقية بعينها ، مع المقادير المختلفة متواردة عليها ، فوجب أن يكون المقدار مغايراً لذات الجسم . قالوا : فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن كون الجسم جسماً ، أمر مغاير لكونه طويلاً عريضاً عميقاً ، فامتنع تعريف الجسم بهذا الحد.
As for magnitude and volume, the essence of body, although not free of them either in external existence or in mental existence, yet it has been established by proof that it is distinct from bodyness. Evidence for this: First: If we take a piece of wax and shape it into different forms, the one bodyness remains the same, while the different magnitudes succeed it. The permanent is distinct from the non-permanent. Second: Bodies are equal in bodyness but different in magnitudes. That by which they share is other than that by which they differ. So bodyness is distinct from magnitudes. Third: It has been established by proof that one body, while its essence remains, can undergo rarefaction and condensation. So here the one body's essence remains the same while different magnitudes follow upon it. Therefore magnitude must be distinct from the body's essence. They said: Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that being a body is distinct from being long, wide, deep, so it is impossible to define body by this limit.

Commentary
This passage is a concise presentation of three proofs that magnitude (miqdar) is distinct from bodyness (jismiyya). The author first notes that while body never lacks magnitude, the two are metaphysically distinct. Proof 1: A piece of wax retains its bodyness while its magnitude changes (e.g., reshaped), so the permanent (bodyness) is distinct from the changing (magnitude). Proof 2: All bodies share bodyness but differ in magnitude; that which is common differs from that which distinguishes. The conclusion: being a body is not equivalent to being long, wide, deep, thus the Mu'tazilite definition fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Philosophers' Three Proofs that Magnitude Differs from Corporeality
Presents the philosophers' three proofs that magnitude is distinct from corporeality, rejecting the standard definition of body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' arguments for distinguishing magnitude from corporeality and why they reject the standard definition of body as 'long, wide, deep'.

Core Idea
Magnitude is an accident, not the essence of body.

The philosophers challenge the common definition of body as 'long, wide, deep' by arguing that magnitude is not part of the essence of corporeality. They present three proofs, drawing on examples like wax, shared properties, and rarefaction, to show that the same body can have varying magnitudes. In this lesson, we will examine their reasoning step by step, starting with the relationship of body to line and surface, then moving to the core proofs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context: body, line, and surface
Read the first part of the passage where the philosophers discuss how body relates to line and surface. Note that body can exist without lines but not without surface in external reality, yet surface is not part of the essence.
They argue that if surface were essential, we could not conceive an infinite body, but we can before its impossibility is proven.
2
Identify the three proofs
The passage then states that magnitude is distinct from corporeality and provides three proofs. Find and label them as Proof 1 (wax), Proof 2 (equality and difference), and Proof 3 (rarefaction/condensation).
3
Work through each proof
For each proof, understand the logical structure: what is the premise and how does it lead to the conclusion that magnitude is other than corporeality? Use the commentary for clarification.
Proof 1 uses a piece of wax; Proof 2 uses shared vs differentiating attributes; Proof 3 uses change in volume while essence remains.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
After studying the proofs, read the concluding sentence: 'being a body is different from being long, wide, deep.' Reflect on why this invalidates the standard definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم فرعوا على هذا الأصل ، وقالوا : إن الجسم قد يوجد في الأعيان منفكاً عن الخط مثل : الكرة المصمتة الخالية عن الحركة . فإن هذا الجسم لا يوجد فيه شيء من الخطوط البتة : وأما السطح فإن الجسم لا ينفك عنه في الأعيان . لأن كل جسم فهو متناهي . وكل متناهي ، فلا بد وأن يحيط به حد واحد ، أو حدود بالفعل وذلك يدل على أن الجسم لا ينفك في الأعيان عن وجود السطح ، إلا أنه قد ينفك عنه في الوجود الذهني . لأنه يمكننا أن نتصور جسما غير متناهي إلى أن يقوم الدليل على امتناعه . ولو كان السطح جزءا من ماهية الجسم ، لامتنع تصور الجسم جسماً، إلا إذا عقلناء متناهياً . لأن تصور الماهية منفكاً عن أجزائها : محال . وأما المقدار والحجمية ، فإن ذات الجسم ، وإن كان لا ينفك عنهما ، لا في الوجود الخارجي، ولا في الوجود الذهني . إلا أنه ثبت بالدليل : أنه مغاير للجسمية . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنا إذا أخذنا قطعة من الشمعة ، وشكلناها بالأشكال المختلفة ، فإن الجسمية الواحدة بعينها باقية ، وأما المقادير المختلفة ، فهي متعاقبة عليها ، والباقي مغاير لما هو غير باق . والثاني : وهو أن الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية ، ومختلفة في المقادير . وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة . فالجسمية مغايرة للمقادير . والثالث : إنه ثبت بالدليل : أن الجسم الواحد مع بقاء ذاته ، يقبل التخلخل والتكاثف. فههنا ذات الجسم الواحد باقية بعينها ، مع المقادير المختلفة متواردة عليها ، فوجب أن يكون المقدار مغايراً لذات الجسم . قالوا : فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن كون الجسم جسماً ، أمر مغاير لكونه طويلاً عريضاً عميقاً ، فامتنع تعريف الجسم بهذا الحد.
Then they branched upon this principle and said: The body may exist in external reality free from line, such as a solid sphere devoid of motion. For this body contains no lines at all. As for the surface, the body is not free from it in external reality, because every body is finite, and every finite thing must be surrounded by one or more actual boundaries, and that indicates that the body is not free from the existence of surface in external reality, except that it may be free from it in mental existence, because we can conceive an infinite body until a proof is established for its impossibility. And if the surface were a part of the essence of body, it would be impossible to conceive body as body unless we conceive it as finite, because conceiving the essence free from its parts is impossible. As for quantity (miqdar) and volume (hajmiyyah), the essence of body is not free from them either in external existence or in mental existence, yet it is established by proof that it is different from corporeality (jismiyyah). And it is indicated by faces: The first: When we take a piece of wax and shape it into different shapes, the one corporeality remains, while the different quantities succeed it, and what remains is different from what does not remain. The second: Bodies are equal in corporeality and differ in quantities, and that by which they share is other than that by which they differ, so corporeality is other than quantities. The third: It is established by proof that one body, with its essence remaining, accepts rarefaction and condensation; here the one essence of the body remains while different quantities succeed it, so quantity must be other than the essence of the body. They said: So it is established by what we have mentioned that being a body is different from being long, wide, deep, so the definition of body by this definition is impossible.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument that the standard definition of body as 'long, wide, deep' is invalid because magnitude is not part of the essence of body. It begins by discussing the relationship of body to line and surface, then presents three proofs for the distinction between corporeality (jismiyyah) and magnitude (miqdar).

The philosophers first note that body can exist without actual lines (e.g., a sphere), but surface is inseparable in external existence, though separable conceptually. If surface were part of the essence, conceiving body without finitude would be impossible—but we can conceive an infinite body before its impossibility is proven. Then they state that magnitude, though inseparable, is proven distinct from corporeality. They give three proofs:
1. A piece of wax retains its corporeality through shape changes, while magnitudes vary; what persists differs from what changes.
2. Bodies share in corporeality but differ in magnitude; what is shared differs from what differentiates.
3. One body can undergo rarefaction and condensation, remaining the same yet accepting different magnitudes, so magnitude is other than the body's essence.
They conclude that 'being a body' is distinct from 'being extended', rendering the standard definition invalid.

Key Terms
جسم (jism)
جسمية (jismiyyah)
مقدار (miqdar)
حجمية (hajmiyyah)
سطح (sath)
خط (khatt)
تخلخل (takhalkhul)
تكاثف (takathuf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Magnitude is part of the essence of body, so body is essentially 'long, wide, deep'.
Correction: The philosophers prove that magnitude is an accident distinct from corporeality because the same body can have different magnitudes while retaining its identity.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof (the wax example), what persists when the wax is reshaped?
A) The magnitude
B) The shape
C) The corporeality
D) The surface

Key Takeaway
The three proofs demonstrate that magnitude is an accident distinct from the essence of corporeality, invalidating the definition of body as 'long, wide, deep'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often define yourself by your size, shape, and physical appearance—but this lesson shows that magnitude is not your essence. What are you if not your body? How will you live today remembering that your true identity is not in how you look but in something deeper?
?
Change in your body—aging, weight loss, illness—can feel like a loss of self. Does this proof challenge that feeling? Will you commit to anchoring your self-worth in what remains constant (your essence) rather than what changes?

The Sphere Lacks No Line: First Refutation
Reiteration of definition and first refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' objection that a sphere has no lines fails under the atomic hypothesis.

Core Idea
Even a sphere has direction; parts align to form a line.

The author first reaffirms that the definition of body as 'long, wide, deep' is correct if bodies are composed of indivisible parts. He then tackles the philosophers' first argument that a sphere lacks lines. He shows that even on atomic theory, the sphere's parts must align in a direction, constituting a line.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow. Notice the initial affirmation and then the response.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
The philosophers claim that a body can exist free from lines, like a sphere. Pinpoint that claim.
3
Follow the refutation
The author argues that on atomic theory, the sphere's parts still meet in a direction, which is a line. Understand why this refutes the claim.
The line is defined as parts meeting in one direction (simt).
4
Connect to the broader context
Consider why this refutation supports the definition of body as long, wide, deep. See how the refutation preserves the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنا بينا : أن بتقدير أن يكون الجسم مركباً من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ ، فإن قولنا : الجسم هو الطويل العريض العميق ، يكون حداً صحيحاً . وهذه السؤالات التي ذكرها الفلاسفة تكون بأسرها باطلة على ذلك التقدير .

وأما قوله : ( الجسم قد يوجد خالياً عن الخط ، مثل الكرة ) فنقول : هذا باطل . لأن بتقدير أن يكون الجسم مركباً من الأجزاء ، فالكرة لا بد وأن يحصل فيها أجزاء مفروضة متلاقية على سمت واحد ، وذلك هو الخط . وأما قوله ثانياً : ( إن السطح غير لازم لماهية الجسم في الذهن ، فوجب أن لا يكون مقوماً لماهيته ) فتقول : هذا يشكل على قولكم : بكون الجسم مركباً من الهيولى والصورة، مع أن الناس يعقلون كون الجسم جسماً ، مع الذهول عن كونه مركباً من الهيولى والصورة . وأما قوله ثالثاً : ( إن الفرق بين الجسمية وبين المقدار ، حاصل من الوجوه الثلاثة ) فنقول : تلك الوجوه بأسرها ضعيفة . أما الأول : فلانا إذا أخذنا الشمعة الواحدة ، وشكلناها بالأشكال المختلفة . فههنا كما أن الجسمية الواحدة باقية بعينها ، فكذلك الحجمية الواحدة ، والمقدار الواحد باقي بعينه . وأما المتبدل المختلف وهو الشكل . فإنه تارة يصير كرة ، وتارة مكعباً ، وتارة على شكل آخر . فالمقدار في كل الأوقات واحد ، وأما المتبدل فهو الأشكال ، وانتقال أجزاء ذلك الجسم من سمت إلى سمت آخر . وأما الوجه الثاني : وهو قوله : ( الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية ، ومختلفة في المقادير ) فنقول : والمقادير أيضاً متساوية في أصل كونها مقادير ، ومختلفة في الكبر والصغر. فيلزم أن يكون للمقدار : مقدار آخر .
And know that we have shown: assuming that the body is composed of indivisible parts, our saying 'the body is the long, wide, deep' is a correct definition. And these objections that the philosophers mentioned are entirely invalid on that assumption.

As for his statement: 'The body may exist free from line, like the sphere'—we say: This is false. For on the assumption that the body is composed of parts, the sphere must necessarily have hypothetical parts meeting in one direction, and that is the line.

As for his second statement: 'The surface is not necessary for the essence of body in the mind, so it must not be constitutive of its essence'—we say: This is problematic on your view that the body is composed of hylemorphic matter and form, while people conceive of body as body while being heedless of its being composed of matter and form.

As for his third statement: 'The distinction between corporeality and magnitude is obtained from three faces'—we say: Those faces are entirely weak. As for the first: When we take the one wax and shape it into different shapes, here just as the one corporeality remains, so too the one volume and the one magnitude remain. What changes and varies is the shape: sometimes it becomes a sphere, sometimes a cube, sometimes another shape. So the magnitude is one at all times, while what changes is the shapes and the transfer of parts of that body from one direction to another.

As for the second face, which is his statement: 'Bodies are equal in corporeality and differ in magnitudes'—we say: Magnitudes are also equal in the fundamental fact of being magnitudes, and differ in bigness and smallness; therefore magnitude would require another magnitude.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. The author begins by reiterating his earlier point: on the atomic theory (composition from indivisible parts), the definition 'body is long, wide, deep' is correct, and the philosophers' objections are invalid.

Then he addresses each of their three arguments in detail:
1. The claim that a sphere has no lines: The author argues that even on atomic theory, the sphere's parts must align in a direction, which constitutes a line.
2. The claim that surface is not mentally necessary for body's essence: The author points out that this would also apply to the philosophers' own view of hylemorphic composition, since people conceive body without thinking of matter and form—undermining their objection.
3. The claim that three proofs distinguish corporeality from magnitude: The author shows each proof is weak.
   - First proof (wax example): He argues that both corporeality and volume/magnitude remain, while only shape changes—so no distinction is shown.
   - Second proof (commonality and difference): He counters that magnitudes also share in being magnitudes while differing in size, which would lead to an infinite regress if that logic were applied.
The refutation demonstrates that the philosophers' arguments fail to prove magnitude is not part of the body's essence, especially under the atomic assumption.

Key Terms
أجزاء لا تتجزأ (ajza' la tatajaza') - indivisible parts
سمت (simt) - direction

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first philosophers' objection that the author refutes?
A) The definition of body is too vague.
B) The body may exist free from line, like a sphere.
C) The body is composed of matter and form.
D) Magnitude is not part of the body's essence.

Key Takeaway
Under atomic theory, even a sphere has lines because its parts align in a direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed an objection as plausible without examining its foundational assumptions, like the philosophers did with the sphere? Where in your own faith or life might you be doing that right now? Take time today to question one assumption you hold about Allah’s creation or your own practice, and see if it withstands deeper scrutiny.
?
The argument of this lesson hinges on the assumption of indivisible parts. Your entire worldview rests on certain foundational beliefs—about Allah, the Qur’an, and yourself. Are you aware of what those assumptions are? Will you commit to learning the proofs that support them, so your faith is built on certainty, not habit?
?
If even a sphere contains hidden lines because of its underlying parts, what does that tell you about the layers of reality? Are you content with surface-level understanding, or will you seek to uncover the deeper structure of truth—even when it challenges your comfortable view?

Surface Not Necessary in Mind: Second Refutation
Second refutation using their own assumptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' second argument (that surface is not mentally necessary for body's essence) is self-defeating.

Core Idea
If surface is not necessary, then matter-form composition is also not mentally necessary.

The philosophers argue that surface is not necessary for the essence of body in the mind, so it should not be a constituent. The author cleverly turns this argument against them: on their own view of matter and form (hylemorphism), people conceive body without thinking of matter and form, so by the same logic their own constituents are not essential.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the philosophers' claim and the author's response.
2
Understand the objection
The philosophers say that since in the mind, body can be conceived without surface, surface is not part of its essence.
This is a mental test for essentiality.
3
Analyze the refutation
The author points out that the same logic applies to matter (hayula) and form (surah). People can conceive body without thinking of matter and form, so those would also be non-essential. This undermines the philosophers' own metaphysics.
The refutation exposes inconsistency.
4
Reflect on the strategy
Note how the author uses reductio ad absurdum against the philosophers' own principles.

Source Text

وأما قوله : ( الجسم قد يوجد خالياً عن الخط ، مثل الكرة ) فنقول : هذا باطل . لأن بتقدير أن يكون الجسم مركباً من الأجزاء ، فالكرة لا بد وأن يحصل فيها أجزاء مفروضة متلاقية على سمت واحد ، وذلك هو الخط . وأما قوله ثانياً : ( إن السطح غير لازم لماهية الجسم في الذهن ، فوجب أن لا يكون مقوماً لماهيته ) فتقول : هذا يشكل على قولكم : بكون الجسم مركباً من الهيولى والصورة، مع أن الناس يعقلون كون الجسم جسماً ، مع الذهول عن كونه مركباً من الهيولى والصورة . وأما قوله ثالثاً : ( إن الفرق بين الجسمية وبين المقدار ، حاصل من الوجوه الثلاثة ) فنقول : تلك الوجوه بأسرها ضعيفة . أما الأول : فلانا إذا أخذنا الشمعة الواحدة ، وشكلناها بالأشكال المختلفة . فههنا كما أن الجسمية الواحدة باقية بعينها ، فكذلك الحجمية الواحدة ، والمقدار الواحد باقي بعينه . وأما المتبدل المختلف وهو الشكل . فإنه تارة يصير كرة ، وتارة مكعباً ، وتارة على شكل آخر . فالمقدار في كل الأوقات واحد ، وأما المتبدل فهو الأشكال ، وانتقال أجزاء ذلك الجسم من سمت إلى سمت آخر . وأما الوجه الثاني : وهو قوله : ( الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية ، ومختلفة في المقادير ) فنقول : والمقادير أيضاً متساوية في أصل كونها مقادير ، ومختلفة في الكبر والصغر. فيلزم أن يكون للمقدار : مقدار آخر .
And know that we have shown: assuming that the body is composed of indivisible parts, our saying 'the body is the long, wide, deep' is a correct definition. And these objections that the philosophers mentioned are entirely invalid on that assumption.

As for his statement: 'The body may exist free from line, like the sphere'—we say: This is false. For on the assumption that the body is composed of parts, the sphere must necessarily have hypothetical parts meeting in one direction, and that is the line.

Distinction Between Corporeality and Magnitude: Third Refutation
Third refutation with two sub-proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the three proofs philosophers use to distinguish corporeality from magnitude are weak.

Core Idea
Magnitude remains constant; only shape changes, so the alleged distinction collapses.

The third philosophers' argument claims three proofs that corporeality and magnitude are distinct. The author shows each proof is weak. The first proof uses the wax example: they claim corporeality remains while magnitude changes. The author shows that magnitude also remains, only shape changes. The second proof concerns equality and difference: bodies equal in corporeality differ in magnitude; the author counters that magnitudes also differ in size, leading to infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the third refutation. It contains two sub-arguments (two faces).
2
First face: wax example
Identify the philosophers' claim: when you reshape wax, corporeality stays but magnitude changes. The author refutes: actually both corporeality and magnitude stay; only shape changes.
The magnitude (volume) remains the same; shape is different.
3
Second face: equality and difference
Philosophers say bodies are equal in corporeality but differ in magnitude. Author responds: magnitudes also are equal in being magnitudes but differ in size, leading to an infinite regress (magnitude needing another magnitude).
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Synthesize the refutation
Notice that both faces show that the philosophers' reasoning leads to contradictions or infinite regress, thus the distinction between corporeality and magnitude is not proven.

وأما قوله : ( الجسم قد يوجد خالياً عن الخط ، مثل الكرة ) فنقول : هذا باطل . لأن بتقدير أن يكون الجسم مركباً من الأجزاء ، فالكرة لا بد وأن يحصل فيها أجزاء مفروضة متلاقية على سمت واحد ، وذلك هو الخط . وأما قوله ثانياً : ( إن السطح غير لازم لماهية الجسم في الذهن ، فوجب أن لا يكون مقوماً لماهيته ) فتقول : هذا يشكل على قولكم : بكون الجسم مركباً من الهيولى والصورة، مع أن الناس يعقلون كون الجسم جسماً ، مع الذهول عن كونه مركباً من الهيولى والصورة . وأما قوله ثالثاً : ( إن الفرق بين الجسمية وبين المقدار ، حاصل من الوجوه الثلاثة ) فنقول : تلك الوجوه بأسرها ضعيفة . أما الأول : فلانا إذا أخذنا الشمعة الواحدة ، وشكلناها بالأشكال المختلفة . فههنا كما أن الجسمية الواحدة باقية بعينها ، فكذلك الحجمية الواحدة ، والمقدار الواحد باقي بعينه . وأما المتبدل المختلف وهو الشكل . فإنه تارة يصير كرة ، وتارة مكعباً ، وتارة على شكل آخر . فالمقدار في كل الأوقات واحد ، وأما المتبدل فهو الأشكال ، وانتقال أجزاء ذلك الجسم من سمت إلى سمت آخر . وأما الوجه الثاني : وهو قوله : ( الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية ، ومختلفة في المقادير ) فنقول : والمقادير أيضاً متساوية في أصل كونها مقادير ، ومختلفة في الكبر والصغر. فيلزم أن يكون للمقدار : مقدار آخر .
And know that we have shown: assuming that the body is composed of indivisible parts, our saying 'the body is the long, wide, deep' is a correct definition. And these objections that the philosophers mentioned are entirely invalid on that assumption.

As for his statement: 'The body may exist free from line, like the sphere'—we say: This is false. For on the assumption that the body is composed of parts, the sphere must necessarily have hypothetical parts meeting in one direction, and that is the line.

As for his second statement: 'The surface is not necessary for the essence of body in the mind, so it must not be constitutive of its essence'—we say: This is problematic on your view that the body is composed of hylemorphic matter and form, while people conceive of body as body while being heedless of its being composed of matter and form.

As for his third statement: 'The distinction between corporeality and magnitude is obtained from three faces'—we say: Those faces are entirely weak. As for the first: When we take the one wax and shape it into different shapes, here just as the one corporeality remains, so too the one volume and the one magnitude remain. What changes and varies is the shape: sometimes it becomes a sphere, sometimes a cube, sometimes another shape. So the magnitude is one at all times, while what changes is the shapes and the transfer of parts of that body from one direction to another.

As for the second face, which is his statement: 'Bodies are equal in corporeality and differ in magnitudes'—we say: Magnitudes are also equal in the fundamental fact of being magnitudes, and differ in bigness and smallness; therefore magnitude would require another magnitude.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. The author begins by reiterating his earlier point: on the atomic theory (composition from indivisible parts), the definition 'body is long, wide, deep' is correct, and the philosophers' objections are invalid.

Then he addresses each of their three arguments in detail:
1. The claim that a sphere has no lines: The author argues that even on atomic theory, the sphere's parts must align in a direction, which constitutes a line.
2. The claim that surface is not mentally necessary for body's essence: The author points out that this would also apply to the philosophers' own view of hylemorphic composition, since people conceive body without thinking of matter and form—undermining their objection.
3. The claim that three proofs distinguish corporeality from magnitude: The author shows each proof is weak.
   - First proof (wax example): He argues that both corporeality and volume/magnitude remain, while only shape changes—so no distinction is shown.
   - Second proof (commonality and difference): He counters that magnitudes also share in being magnitudes while differing in size, which would lead to an infinite regress if that logic were applied.
The refutation demonstrates that the philosophers' arguments fail to prove magnitude is not part of the body's essence, especially under the atomic assumption.

Key Terms
شكل (shakl) - shape
تخلخل (takhalkhul) - rarefaction
تكاثف (takathuf) - condensation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Corporeality and magnitude are distinct because changing shape changes magnitude.
Correction: Changing shape does not change magnitude (volume); shape and magnitude are different properties.

Checkpoint
In the first face of the third argument, what does the author say remains constant?
A) The shape
B) The corporeality and magnitude
C) The direction
D) The parts

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' proofs for distinguishing corporeality from magnitude are weak because they confuse shape with magnitude or lead to infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at changes in your life—circumstances, appearance, status—do you tend to confuse these superficial changes with a change in your fundamental identity as a servant of Allah? How can you keep your core beliefs constant like the magnitude in the wax example?
?
The author refutes seemingly clever arguments by pointing out logical flaws. Are there any beliefs or practices you hold that you have not examined critically? Will you commit to studying a foundational Islamic creed text to ensure your beliefs rest on solid proof?
?
This lesson shows that weak arguments can be exposed by careful analysis. Have you ever accepted a religious argument without checking its foundations? What step will you take today to strengthen your understanding of the proofs for your faith?

Refuting the Body-Without-Line Objection
Refutes the claim that a sphere has no line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that a sphere exists without a line, thereby defending the definition of body as having length, width, and depth.

Core Idea
Even a sphere must have parts aligned in one direction, forming a line.

This lesson tackles a specific objection against the classical definition of a body as something with three dimensions. The opponent argues that a sphere is a body yet has no visible lines. But the author shows that on analysis, a sphere necessarily contains hypothetical parts that align along a single direction, which constitutes a line. Let's examine the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
First, read the opponent's claim and the author's immediate rebuttal. Note the structure: first the objection is stated, then the refutation begins with 'we say: This is false.'
Pay attention to the logical connector 'because' that introduces the proof.
2
Understand the key assumption
The author assumes the body is composed of parts. On this assumption, even a sphere must have hypothetical parts meeting along one direction.
The term 'hypothetical parts' (أجزاء مفروضة) is crucial.
3
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is that this alignment of parts is what is meant by a line. Therefore the sphere does contain a line, and the objection fails.
This reasoning reinforces the definition of body as having length, width, and depth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله : ( الجسم قد يوجد خالياً عن الخط ، مثل الكرة فنقول : هذا باطل . لان بتقدير أن يكون الجسم مركباً من الأجزاء ، فالكرة لا بد وأن يحصل فيها أجزاء مفروضة متلاقية على سمت واحد ، وذلك هو الخط .
And as for his statement: 'The body may exist devoid of a line, like the sphere,' we say: This is false. Because on the assumption that the body is composed of parts, the sphere must contain hypothetical parts meeting along one direction, and that is the line.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the first objection against the definition of body as long, wide, deep. The opponent argues that a sphere exists without a line. The author responds that even a sphere, on the assumption of being composed of parts, must have parts aligned in a single direction, which constitutes a line. Thus the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
جسم
خط
أجزاء
كرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A sphere has no lines at all.
Correction: Even a sphere, when analyzed as composed of parts, has hypothetical parts aligned in one direction, which is a line.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that a sphere has no line?
A) By saying the sphere is not a body.
B) By arguing that the sphere must contain parts aligned in one direction, which is a line.
C) By stating that lines are imaginary.
D) By denying the existence of spheres.

Key Takeaway
The sphere must contain hypothetical parts aligned in one direction, which is a line, so the objection fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that something is simple (like a sphere having no line) only to find that reality is more complex? In what area of your faith or practice might you be making such an assumption right now, and what would it cost you to ignore that complexity?
?
The opponent relied on an apparent observation (a sphere looks line-free) to reject a deeper truth. Where in your own life does your immediate perception lead you to deny a truth that careful reflection would affirm? Will you choose on-the-surface impressions over reasoned conviction?
?
This refutation shows that even a sphere, which seems line-free, contains a line by necessity. What necessities of your deen have you been avoiding because they don't 'fit' your current mental model? Commit to one such necessity you will re-examine today.

Refuting the Claim that Surface is Not Constitutive
Refutes the second objection by showing it applies equally to the opponent's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the second objection against the lability theory by turning the argument against the opponent's own view.

Core Idea
An objection that would also refute the opponent's own view is self-undermining.

The second objection claims that because surface is not always present in the mental conception of a body, it cannot be a constitutive element of its quiddity. The author responds by pointing out that the same reasoning would apply to the opponent's own theory that body is composed of matter and form, since people conceive of a body without being aware of this composition. Thus the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the opponent's premise: surface is not necessary for the mental quiddity of body, so it must not be constitutive. Understand the logical structure.
Notice the key term 'lāzim' (necessary) in the sense of inseparable.
2
Read the author's refutation
The author says this same reasoning would be problematic for the opponent's own view. Why? Because people conceive of body without awareness of matter and form.
This is a classic tu quoque (you, too) argument.
3
Reflect on the strength of the refutation
If the opponent's own standard for constitution is consistency with common conception, then their own theory fails by that standard. Therefore the objection is not valid.
The argument does not prove that surface is constitutive, but it invalidates the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ثانياً : ( إن السطح غير لازم لماهية الجسم في الذهن ، فوجب أن لا يكون مقوماً لماهيته ، فتقول : هذا يشكل على قولكم : بكون الجسم مركباً من الهيولى والصورة، مع أن الناس يعقلون كون الجسم جسماً ، مع الذهول عن كونه مركباً من الهيولى والصورة .
And as for his second statement: 'The surface is not necessary for the quiddity of the body in the mind, so it must not be constitutive of its quiddity,' we say: This is problematic for your own view that the body is composed of matter and form, while people conceive of a body as a body while being oblivious to its composition of matter and form.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage refutes the second objection that surface is not constitutive of body's quiddity. The author points out that the same reasoning would undermine the opponent's own theory that body is composed of matter and form, since people conceive of body without being aware of that composition. Thus the objection is self-undermining.

Key Terms
سطح
ماهية
هيولى
صورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a property (like surface) is not explicitly present in one's mental concept of a thing, it cannot be part of that thing's essence.
Correction: The author shows that this standard would also rule out the opponent's own theory of composition, so the standard is not valid.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the second objection is problematic for the opponent?
A) Because the opponent denies the existence of surfaces.
B) Because the opponent's own theory of matter and form is also not apparent to common understanding.
C) Because the objector admits surface is unnecessary.
D) Because the author agrees with the objector.

Key Takeaway
An objection that also refutes the opponent's own position is not a valid objection; it is self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you criticize someone's belief, do you first check whether your own belief passes the same test? Is there a conviction you hold that you have never subjected to the same standard you apply to others? Will you examine one such conviction today?
?
What belief of yours would collapse if you applied the logic of this lesson to it—that an argument that would also refute your own position cannot be valid? Are you willing to adjust your stance if you find such an inconsistency?
?
If someone pointed out that your argument against them could be turned against you, would you be humble enough to reconsider, or would you defend your position out of pride? How can you build the habit of self-critique before attacking others?

Refutation of the First Aspect: Shape Change and Magnitude Constancy
Refutes the first aspect of the third objection using the candle example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the example of a single candle changing shape to refute the claim that magnitude is distinct from body-ness.

Core Idea
Shape changes do not alter magnitude; they only rearrange parts.

The third objection claims that body and magnitude are distinct based on three aspects. The first aspect argues that a body's shape can change while its body-ness remains, implying magnitude is separate. Here, the author refutes this using the simple example of a candle that remains the same in magnitude despite shape changes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the first aspect
Read the Arabic passage dealing with the first aspect and its translation. Notice how the objector uses the candle example.
2
Identify the key elements in the candle example
Find the elements that remain constant (body-ness, volume, magnitude) and what changes (shape).
3
Understand the refutation logic
The author argues that since magnitude remains the same, there is no need to posit magnitude as separate from body-ness. The change is only in shape, not in magnitude.
This directly refutes the objector's first aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ثالثاً : « إن الفرق بين الجسمية وبين المقدار ، حاصل من الوجوه الثلاثة ، فنقول : تلك الوجوه بأسرها ضعيفة . أما الأول : فلانا إذا أخذنا الشمعة الواحدة ، وشكلناها بالأشكال المختلفة . فههنا الجسمية الواحدة باقية بعينها ، فكذلك الحجمية الواحدة ، والمقدار الواحد باقي بعينه . وأما المتبدل المختلف وهو الشكل . فإنه تارة يصير كرة ، وتارة مكعباً ، وتارة على شكل آخر فالمقدار في كل الأوقات واحد ، وأما المتبدل فهو الأشكال ، وانتقال أجزاء ذلك الجسم من سمت إلى سمت آخر . وأما الوجه الثاني : وهو قوله : ( الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية ، ومختلفة في المقادير ، فنقول : والمقادير أيضاً متساوية في أصل كونها مقادير ، ومختلفة في الكبر والصغر. فيلزم أن يكون للمقدار : مقدار آخر . وأما الوجه الثالث : فهو بناء على أن الذات الواحدة ، قد يختلف مقدارها بالصغر والكبر ، مع بقاء تلك الذات بعينها . وقد دللنا بالبراهين القاطعة في باب الحركة : على أن ذلك محال . فثبت : بهذه البيانات أن الوجوه التي عولوا عليها في إبطال قول من قال : [ الجسم ] هو الطويل العريض العميق : أقوال باطلة [ والله أعلم ] .
And as for his third statement: 'The difference between jismiyyah and magnitude is obtained from three aspects,' we say: Those aspects are all weak. As for the first: Because if we take one candle and form it into different shapes, here the single jismiyyah remains the same, likewise the single volume and the single magnitude remain the same. As for what changes and differs, it is the shape. Sometimes it becomes a sphere, sometimes a cube, sometimes another shape, but the magnitude is one at all times; what changes is the shapes and the transfer of parts of that body from one direction to another. As for the second aspect: His statement that bodies are equal in jismiyyah but differ in magnitudes, we say: Magnitudes are also equal in being magnitudes but differ in bigness and smallness, so magnitude would require another magnitude. As for the third aspect: It is based on the idea that the same essence may have a magnitude varying in smallness and bigness while the essence itself remains the same. We have demonstrated with decisive proofs in the chapter on motion that this is impossible. So it is established by these expositions that the aspects they relied upon to invalidate the statement of those who say [the body] is long, wide, deep are false statements, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage refutes the third objection that there is a distinction between jismiyyah (body-ness) and magnitude (miqdar). The objector presents three aspects: (1) body remains same while shape changes, so magnitude is distinct; (2) bodies equal in bodyness differ in magnitude; (3) same essence can have different magnitudes. The author refutes each: (1) the same volume and magnitude remain through shape changes; (2) the same reasoning applied to magnitudes would require an infinite regress; (3) he cites his own proof from the chapter on motion that an essence cannot change in magnitude while remaining the same. Thus the third objection fails.

Key Terms
جسمية
مقدار
شكل
حجم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that changing shape proves magnitude is distinct from body-ness.
Correction: The author argues that magnitude itself remains constant across shape changes; only the shape changes, so magnitude is not proven to be separate.

Checkpoint
In the author's candle example, what remains unchanged?
A) The shape
B) The magnitude and volume
C) The direction of parts
D) The body-ness only

Key Takeaway
The first aspect fails because magnitude remains constant through shape changes, so it does not support a distinction between body-ness and magnitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever mistaken a change in someone's 'shape'—their status, appearance, or role—for a change in their intrinsic worth? How does this lesson's truth about magnitude constancy challenge you to see others differently starting today?
?
When your own circumstances 'reshaped' into something unfamiliar, did you feel like your core identity had changed? What would it mean for you to live with the conviction that your jismiyyah—your essential self before Allah—remains constant, even when life forces you into a different shape?
?
Your deeds are like the candle's shape—they change, but your soul's reality endures. Are you more concerned with altering your 'shape' to fit worldly approval, or are you nurturing the substance that remains? What immediate step will you take to protect your inner constancy?

Refutation of the Second Aspect: Infinite Regress in Magnitudes
Refutes the second aspect using a regress argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses an infinite regress argument to refute the claim that differences in magnitude prove magnitude is distinct from body-ness.

Core Idea
If differences in magnitude require a separate magnitude, an infinite regress follows.

The second aspect of the objection states that bodies are equal in body-ness but differ in magnitude, suggesting magnitude is an added property. The author refutes this by applying the same logic to magnitudes themselves, leading to an infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second aspect
Read the Arabic and translation for the second aspect. Note the objector's claim and the author's response.
2
Understand the objector's argument
The objector says: bodies share jismiyyah but have different magnitudes, so magnitude must be extra.
3
Analyze the author's counter-argument
The author applies the same reasoning to magnitudes: magnitudes are equal in being magnitudes but differ in size, so they too would need another magnitude, leading to infinite regress.
This shows the logic is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ثالثاً : « إن الفرق بين الجسمية وبين المقدار ، حاصل من الوجوه الثلاثة ، فنقول : تلك الوجوه بأسرها ضعيفة . أما الأول : فلانا إذا أخذنا الشمعة الواحدة ، وشكلناها بالأشكال المختلفة . فههنا الجسمية الواحدة باقية بعينها ، فكذلك الحجمية الواحدة ، والمقدار الواحد باقي بعينه . وأما المتبدل المختلف وهو الشكل . فإنه تارة يصير كرة ، وتارة مكعباً ، وتارة على شكل آخر فالمقدار في كل الأوقات واحد ، وأما المتبدل فهو الأشكال ، وانتقال أجزاء ذلك الجسم من سمت إلى سمت آخر . وأما الوجه الثاني : وهو قوله : ( الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية ، ومختلفة في المقادير ، فنقول : والمقادير أيضاً متساوية في أصل كونها مقادير ، ومختلفة في الكبر والصغر. فيلزم أن يكون للمقدار : مقدار آخر . وأما الوجه الثالث : فهو بناء على أن الذات الواحدة ، قد يختلف مقدارها بالصغر والكبر ، مع بقاء تلك الذات بعينها . وقد دللنا بالبراهين القاطعة في باب الحركة : على أن ذلك محال . فثبت : بهذه البيانات أن الوجوه التي عولوا عليها في إبطال قول من قال : [ الجسم ] هو الطويل العريض العميق : أقوال باطلة [ والله أعلم ] .
And as for his third statement: 'The difference between jismiyyah and magnitude is obtained from three aspects,' we say: Those aspects are all weak. As for the first: Because if we take one candle and form it into different shapes, here the single jismiyyah remains the same, likewise the single volume and the single magnitude remain the same. As for what changes and differs, it is the shape. Sometimes it becomes a sphere, sometimes a cube, sometimes another shape, but the magnitude is one at all times; what changes is the shapes and the transfer of parts of that body from one direction to another. As for the second aspect: His statement that bodies are equal in jismiyyah but differ in magnitudes, we say: Magnitudes are also equal in being magnitudes but differ in bigness and smallness, so magnitude would require another magnitude. As for the third aspect: It is based on the idea that the same essence may have a magnitude varying in smallness and bigness while the essence itself remains the same. We have demonstrated with decisive proofs in the chapter on motion that this is impossible. So it is established by these expositions that the aspects they relied upon to invalidate the statement of those who say [the body] is long, wide, deep are false statements, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage refutes the third objection that there is a distinction between jismiyyah (body-ness) and magnitude (miqdar). The objector presents three aspects: (1) body remains same while shape changes, so magnitude is distinct; (2) bodies equal in bodyness differ in magnitude; (3) same essence can have different magnitudes. The author refutes each: (1) the same volume and magnitude remain through shape changes; (2) the same reasoning applied to magnitudes would require an infinite regress; (3) he cites his own proof from the chapter on motion that an essence cannot change in magnitude while remaining the same. Key Terms

جسمية
كبر
صغر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that differences in magnitude among bodies prove magnitude is distinct from body-ness.
Correction: The author shows that the same reasoning applied to magnitudes leads to infinite regress, so the argument is invalid.

Checkpoint
What problem does the author identify in the objector's second aspect?
A) It contradicts the first aspect
B) It leads to an infinite regress
C) It assumes magnitudes are equal
D) It ignores the motion proof

Key Takeaway
The second aspect is refuted by showing that the same logic would require an infinite chain of magnitudes, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Refutation of the Third Aspect: Magnitude Change and Motion
Refutes the third aspect by citing motion proofs against magnitude change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author invokes proofs from the chapter on motion to refute the claim that the same essence can have different magnitudes.

Core Idea
An essence cannot change in magnitude while remaining the same; motion proofs show this impossibility.

The third aspect of the objection argues that the same essence can have different magnitudes (e.g., growing larger or smaller). The author responds by citing decisive proofs from the chapter on motion that such a change is impossible while the essence remains identical.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the third aspect
Read the Arabic and translation for the third aspect. Note the objector's claim and the author's reference to motion proofs.
2
Understand the objector's claim
The objector says the same essence can have a magnitude that varies in size, implying magnitude is not essential.
3
Grasp the author's refutation
The author states that he has already proven in the chapter on motion that an essence cannot change in magnitude while remaining the same essence. This refutes the claim.
The author does not re-prove it here but relies on prior proofs.

وأما قوله ثالثاً : « إن الفرق بين الجسمية وبين المقدار ، حاصل من الوجوه الثلاثة ، فنقول : تلك الوجوه بأسرها ضعيفة . أما الأول : فلانا إذا أخذنا الشمعة الواحدة ، وشكلناها بالأشكال المختلفة . فههنا الجسمية الواحدة باقية بعينها ، فكذلك الحجمية الواحدة ، والمقدار الواحد باقي بعينه . وأما المتبدل المختلف وهو الشكل . فإنه تارة يصير كرة ، وتارة مكعباً ، وتارة على شكل آخر فالمقدار في كل الأوقات واحد ، وأما المتبدل فهو الأشكال ، وانتقال أجزاء ذلك الجسم من سمت إلى سمت آخر . وأما الوجه الثاني : وهو قوله : ( الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية ، ومختلفة في المقادير ، فنقول : والمقادير أيضاً متساوية في أصل كونها مقادير ، ومختلفة في الكبر والصغر. فيلزم أن يكون للمقدار : مقدار آخر . وأما الوجه الثالث : فهو بناء على أن الذات الواحدة ، قد يختلف مقدارها بالصغر والكبر ، مع بقاء تلك الذات بعينها . وقد دللنا بالبراهين القاطعة في باب الحركة : على أن ذلك محال . فثبت : بهذه البيانات أن الوجوه التي عولوا عليها في إبطال قول من قال : [ الجسم ] هو الطويل العريض العميق : أقوال باطلة [ والله أعلم ] .
And as for his third statement: 'The difference between jismiyyah and magnitude is obtained from three aspects,' we say: Those aspects are all weak. As for the first: Because if we take one candle and form it into different shapes, here the single jismiyyah remains the same, likewise the single volume and the single magnitude remain the same. As for what changes and differs, it is the shape. Sometimes it becomes a sphere, sometimes a cube, sometimes another shape, but the magnitude is one at all times; what changes is the shapes and the transfer of parts of that body from one direction to another. As for the second aspect: His statement that bodies are equal in jismiyyah but differ in magnitudes, we say: Magnitudes are also equal in being magnitudes but differ in bigness and smallness, so magnitude would require another magnitude. As for the third aspect: It is based on the idea that the same essence may have a magnitude varying in smallness and bigness while the essence itself remains the same. We have demonstrated with decisive proofs in the chapter on motion that this is impossible. So it is established by these expositions that the aspects they relied upon to invalidate the statement of those who say [the body] is long, wide, deep are false statements, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage refutes the third objection that there is a distinction between jismiyyah (body-ness) and magnitude (miqdar). The objector presents three aspects: (1) body remains same while shape changes, so magnitude is distinct; (2) bodies equal in bodyness differ in magnitude; (3) same essence can have different magnitudes. The author refutes each: (1) the same volume and magnitude remain through shape changes; (2) the same reasoning applied to magnitudes would require an infinite regress; (3) he cites his own proof from the chapter on motion that an essence cannot change in magnitude while remaining the same. Thus the third objection fails.

Correction: The author argues, based on motion proofs, that essence and magnitude are inseparable; a change in magnitude implies a change in essence.

Pre-existence of Dimensions in the Body
Proves that the three dimensions must pre-exist in body, supporting its definition as long, wide, deep.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that the three dimensions must pre-exist in a body, supporting the definition of body as long, wide, deep.

Core Idea
The three dimensions must be real and pre-existing, not mental constructs.

In this passage, the proponents of the definition of body as long, wide, deep argue that these dimensions cannot be mere products of our imagination. They insist that the dimensions must have existed before any mental supposition, otherwise the body itself would not exist. This lesson traces their reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setting
Recognize that the definition of body as possessing three dimensions is being defended against the objection that dimensions are mentally imposed.
The opponents may claim dimensions only arise when we suppose them.
2
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the two possibilities presented: dimensions exist only at supposition or they pre-exist.
The author declares the first possibility false and gives proofs.
3
Follow the first proof
Trace the argument: if a specific extension did not exist before supposition, then the body itself would not have existed, because the body is that extension. This is absurd.
This proof relies on the identity of body and extension.
4
Note the second proof (incomplete)
The second proof begins: indication of a thing requires the indicated to already exist. The argument is cut off due to page break.
The author's full reasoning continues in subsequent pages.

Then the proponents of the correctness of this definition argued, saying: When you defined the body as that in which it is not possible to suppose these three dimensions, you have conceded that these three dimensions may occur in it upon supposition. So if we suppose these three dimensions in the body, these lines and extensions that we indicated upon supposition — either it is said that they did not exist before this supposition and only came to be at the time of this supposition, or it is said that they existed before this supposition and will remain existing after this supposition. The first is false, and there are proofs for it: The first: This line is this particular extension, and there is no doubt that this extension existed before the supposition of those who suppose and the consideration of those who consider; otherwise it would necessarily be said that this extension never existed and only occurs now. And if no extension existed before this supposition, then it would be necessary to say that this specific body did not exist before this supposition, because there is no meaning to this body except this thing extended in the three dimensions. It is known that the statement that this body only occurs at the occurrence of this indication is a false statement. The second: Indication of a thing is conditional on the existence of the indicated first, so the indication of the particular extension in this sphere would necessitate being conditional on the existence of...

Commentary
This passage presents the proof of those who affirm the definition of body as long, wide, deep. They argue from the opponent's own admission that the three dimensions can be supposed in body. They then propose two possibilities: either the dimensions exist only during the supposition or they pre-exist. The first is false. The first proof: a specific extension (line) must have existed before the supposition, otherwise the body itself would not have existed since the body is nothing but extended thing. The second proof begins but is incomplete: indication of a thing requires the indicated to exist first, so indication of extension requires extension to exist prior. The argument is cut off at the end of the page.

Proof that Extension is Not Generated by Pointing
Author refutes claim that extension is generated by pointing, providing two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dimensions of a body are inherent and cannot be produced by human pointing or mental supposition.

Core Idea
Pointing does not create extension; extension makes pointing possible.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a rational argument that the three dimensions of a body (length, width, depth) are not generated by our acts of pointing or mental supposition. Instead, these dimensions are intrinsic to the body itself. The author offers two concise proofs to refute the opposing view, establishing the foundation for the correct definition of body.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opposing view
The passage refutes the idea that dimensions arise from pointing. Recognize what is being denied.
The opposing view claims that pointing generates extension.
2
Examine the first argument: circularity
Identify the argument that if extension depends on pointing, then pointing depends on extension, leading to circularity (dawr).
Circularity is impossible in rational proofs.
3
Examine the second argument: absurd consequence
Notice the argument that if pointing produced cuts in a celestial body, we would be acting on its substance, which is absurd.
This underscores that pointing cannot affect the body's essence.
4
Grasp the conclusion: dimensions are intrinsic
Understand that once these arguments invalidate the opposing view, the definition of body as long, wide, deep stands.
Dimensions exist prior to any human supposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك الامتداد في تلك الكرة . فلو قلنا : بأن حصول ذلك الامتداد ، في تلك الكرة ، معلل بهذه الإشارة ، لزم افتقار كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر ، وهو دور ، والدور محال . والثالث : وهو أنا إذا أشرنا إلى جسم الفلك فلو كانت إشارتنا إليه ، تقتضي حدوث خطوط وقطوع فيه ، لزم كوننا متصرفين في جوهر الفلك بالتقطيع والتشكيل . وذلك في غاية البعد. واعلم : أنه ستجيء وجوه كثيرة تقوى ما ذكرناه في [ مسألة ] الجوهر الفرد ، في إبطال قول من يقول إن هذه الامتدادات وهذه الأبعاد ، إنما تحدث في الجسم ، بسبب إشارات المشيرين ، وفروض الفارضين . وإذا بطل هذا ، ثبت أنها كانت موجودة قبل حصول الفرض والتقدير ، وحينئذ يصح قولنا : إن الجسم هو الطويل العريض العميق . والله أعلم .
That extension in that sphere. If we said that the occurrence of that extension in that sphere is caused by this pointing, then each of them would need the other, which is circularity, and circularity is impossible. The third: that if we point to a celestial body, and if our pointing to it necessitated the occurrence of lines and sections in it, then we would be agents acting upon the substance of the celestial body by cutting and shaping. That is extremely remote. And know: that many arguments will come that strengthen what we have mentioned regarding the issue of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), in refuting the statement of those who say that these extensions and these dimensions only occur in the body due to the pointers' pointing and the supposers' suppositions. And if this is invalidated, it is established that they existed before the occurrence of supposition and estimation, and then our statement that the body is long, wide, deep is correct. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof that the extension (imtidad) and dimensions of a body are not generated by human pointing or mental supposition, but are inherent to the body itself. The author offers two arguments. First: if we claim that the extension in a sphere is caused by our pointing to it, then the extension would depend on the pointing and the pointing would depend on the extension (since pointing presupposes an existing extension to point at). This mutual dependence is circular (dawr), which is impossible. Second: if our pointing to a celestial body (jism al-falak) actually produced lines and cuts in it, then we would be manipulating the very substance (jawhar) of that celestial body, creating new divisions and shapes—something clearly absurd. The author then notes that further proofs will be given in the context of the atom (al-jawhar al-fard) to refute the view that extensions and dimensions arise only through acts of pointing (isharat al-mushirin) and mental suppositions (furud al-faridin). Once this view is refuted, it is established that these dimensions existed prior to any human supposition or estimation. Consequently, the definition of body as 'long, wide, deep' (al-tawil al-arid al-amiq) is valid, because these dimensions are intrinsic, not contingent on our mental acts.

Correction: The argument shows dimensions are pre-existing and pointing merely indicates them; dimensions are intrinsic to the body.

Key Takeaway
