Definition of al-Fard (the Fourth Level of Parts)
Defines the fourth level of parts and explains the difference between the four levels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-fard (the hypothetical part) and how it differs from other levels of parts in the kalam theory of atomism.

Core Idea
Al-fard is so small even imagination fails, yet it still admits hypothetical division.

In this passage, the author continues the classification of parts by defining the fourth and final level: al-fard. This part is so minute that the imaginative faculty cannot picture it, yet it remains divisible in theory. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the kalam position on the composition of bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Focus on the definition of al-fard and the contrast between imaginary and hypothetical division.
The passage is dense but builds on the previous levels.
2
Identify key properties of al-fard
Note that al-fard is characterized by being beyond the reach of imagination, yet still having distinguishable sides. This allows hypothetical division.
Compare with imaginary division which requires the part to be conceivable.
3
Connect to the broader doctrine
The passage concludes that this explanation clarifies the difference between four aspects and supports the doctrine of finite parts. Ask yourself how al-fard fits into the atomic theory.
This ties back to the kalam argument for atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يليه في المرتبة الرابعة : الفرض . وهو الجزء الذي يبلغ في الصغر إلى حيث يعجز الوهم عن تخيله وتصوره. وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع حصول القسمة الوهمية فيه لأن الشيء الذي لا يصل الوهم والخيال إلى تصوره ، فإنه يمتنع حصول القسمة الوهمية فيه إلا أنه يكون قابلا للقسمة الفرضية . فإن الوهم والخيال ، وإن عجزا عن إدراكه وتصوره ، إلا أنه في نفسه موصوف بكونه بحيث يتميز أحد جانبيه عن الجانب الثاني. فهذا هو بيان الفرق بين هذه الوجوه الأربعة . وهذا هو شرح مذهب القائلين بكون الجسم مركباً من أجزاء متناهية ، كل واحد منها لا يقبل القسمة البتة.
Then in the fourth rank follows: al-fard (the hypothetical part). It is the part that reaches such smallness that the imagination (wahm) is incapable of conceiving and picturing it. When that is the case, imaginary division is prevented from occurring in it, because a thing which the imagination and fantasy cannot conceive – imaginary division is prevented from occurring in it. However, it is receptive to hypothetical division (qisma fardiyya). For the imagination and fantasy, although incapable of perceiving and conceiving it, yet in itself it is described as being such that one of its sides is distinguished from the second side. This is the explanation of the difference between these four aspects. And this is the explanation of the doctrine of those who say that the body is composed of finite parts, each one of which does not admit of division at all.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth level of parts (al-fard) and explains its distinction from the other three levels. The author begins by stating that the fourth rank is al-fard, which is a part so small that the imaginative faculty (wahm) cannot conceive or picture it. Because of this, imaginary division (qisma wahmiyya) cannot be applied to it, since that division requires the part to be imaginable. However, this part is still receptive to hypothetical division (qisma fardiyya), because in itself it has distinguishable sides, even if the imagination cannot grasp them. The author then concludes that this explanation clarifies the difference between the four aspects (al-wujuh al-arba'a) and that this is the doctrine of those who hold that the body is composed of finite parts (ajza' mutanahiya) each indivisible.

Key Terms
فرد
وهم
قسمة فرضية
قسمة وهمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-fard is the same as the indivisible atom (al-juz' alladhi la yatajaza).
Correction: Al-fard is a product of hypothetical division, while the indivisible atom is the actual ultimate part that cannot be divided at all.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-fard is a theoretically postulated part so fine that imagination cannot grasp it, yet it retains hypothetical divisibility, consistent with the atomic theory of finite parts.

Can an Atom Touch Two Others?
First branch: Can an atom be adjacent to two others?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement among early kalam scholars regarding whether a single indivisible atom can be placed contiguous to two other atoms simultaneously.

Core Idea
Atomists disagreed: Can one atom touch two atoms at once?

The first branch of the atomist view examines a seemingly simple question: can a single indivisible atom be adjacent to two others? This question has implications for how atoms combine to form bodies. Al-Jubba'i and al-Ash'ari said no, while Abu Hashim and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar said yes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage closely
Read the Arabic and translation for the first branch. Notice the positions of the two camps.
The issue is whether a single atom can be 'on' (contiguous to) what is connected to two parts.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify who rejected it (al-Jubba'i and al-Ash'ari) and who permitted it (Abu Hashim and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar).
These are early figures in the kalam tradition.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it affects how atoms are thought to combine into extended bodies.
If an atom can touch two others, it can be a junction point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا ضبط المذاهب الممكنة في هذا الباب . ولتذكر الآن تفاريع كل واحد من هذه الأقسام : أما تفريعات القسم الأول : وهو قول المثبتين للجزء الذي لا يتجزأ فهي أشياء : الفرع الأول : اختلفوا في أنه هل يعقل وقوع الجزء الواحد على متصل الجزءين ؟ فأباه الجبائي و الأشعري : وجوزه أبو هاشم و القاضي عبد الجبار . والفرع الثاني : إن الجوهر الفرد، هل له شكل أم لا ؟ فأباه الأشعري ، وأما أكثر المعتزلة فقد أثبتوا له شكلا . ثم اختلفوا . فمنهم من قال : إنه أشبه بالمثلث . والأكثرون قالوا : إنه أشبه بالمربع . والحق : أنهم شبهوه بالمكعب . لأنهم أثبتوا له جوانب ستة . وزعموا : أنه يمكن أن تحصل به جواهر ستة ، من جوانب ستة . وعلى هذا التقدير ، فإنه يجب أن يكون شكله : بالمكعب . والفرع الثالث : إن الجوهر الواحد. هل له حظ من الأطوال والعروض ؟ فالكل أنكروه ، إلا أبا الحسين الصالحي ، من قدماء المعتزلة . فإنه زعم : أنه لا بد وأن يحصل له قدر من الطول والعرض والعمق . والفرع الرابع : إن الجوهر الفرد . هل يقبل الحياة ، وسائر الأعراض المشروطة بالحياة ، كالعلم والإرادة والقدرة ؟ الأشعري ، وجماعة من قدماء المعتزلة قالوا به. والمتأخرون من المعتزلة أنكروه . وهذه هي المسألة المشهورة في الفرع الرابع.
This is the recording of the possible doctrines in this subject. And let us now recall the subdivisions of each of these divisions. As for the subdivisions of the first division: it is the statement of those who affirm the part that is indivisible. They are matters: The first branch: They disagreed about whether it is conceivable that a single part can be placed contiguous to two parts? Al-Jubba'i and al-Ash'ari rejected it, while Abu Hashim and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar permitted it. The second branch: Whether the individual atom (al-jawhar al-fard) has a shape? Al-Ash'ari denied it, but most of the Mu'tazila affirmed a shape for it. Then they disagreed. Some of them said it resembles a triangle, while most said it resembles a square. The truth is that they likened it to a cube, because they affirmed for it six sides. And they claimed that six atoms can be attached to it from six sides. On this assumption, its shape must be cubical. The third branch: Whether the individual atom has any portion of length and width? All denied it except Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi, from the early Mu'tazila. He claimed that it must necessarily possess a certain amount of length, width, and depth. The fourth branch: Whether the individual atom is receptive to life (hayat) and other accidents conditional upon life, such as knowledge, will, and power? Al-Ash'ari and a group of early Mu'tazila affirmed it, while the later Mu'tazila denied it. And this is the well-known issue concerning the fourth branch.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents four branches (furu') of the first view (the atomist position that the body is composed of indivisible parts). The author first introduces the subdivisions of this view. The first branch concerns whether an indivisible atom can be placed contiguous to two other atoms simultaneously. Al-Jubba'i and al-Ash'ari denied this possibility, while Abu Hashim and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar permitted it. The second branch asks whether the individual atom has a shape. Al-Ash'ari denied it has any shape, but most Mu'tazila affirmed it and debated the specific shape: some said triangle, most said square, but the correct view according to them is a cube, because they held that an atom has six sides, allowing six atoms to attach from six directions. The third branch asks whether the atom has any share of length and width. All denied it except Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi, who insisted that an atom must have some dimension in length, width, and depth. The fourth branch asks whether the atom is receptive to life and life-contingent accidents like knowledge, will, and power. Al-Ash'ari and early Mu'tazila affirmed it, later Mu'tazila denied it. The author notes this as the well-known issue concerning the fourth branch.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ
وقوع
متصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All atomists agreed on how atoms combine.
Correction: This passage shows that early atomists disagreed on fundamental properties like whether an atom can contact two others simultaneously.

B) Al-Jubba'i and al-Ash'ari

Key Takeaway
The first branch of atomist subdivisions concerns whether an atom can touch two others, with al-Jubba'i and al-Ash'ari denying it and Abu Hashim and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar affirming it.

Does the Atom Have a Shape?
Second branch: Does the atom have a shape?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether the individual atom has a shape, and the proposed shapes among different groups.

Core Idea
Does an atom have a shape? Triangle, square, or cube?

The second branch asks whether the individual atom (al-jawhar al-fard) has a shape. Al-Ash'ari denied it, but most Mu'tazila affirmed it. Among those who affirmed shape, there was further disagreement: some said triangle, most said square, but the author notes that the correct view is a cube, based on the claim that atoms have six sides.

Guided Path
1
Read the second branch passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the three positions: no shape, triangle, square, and the conclusion of cube.
The text says 'the truth is that they likened it to a cube'.
2
Identify the positions
List who held which view: al-Ash'ari said no shape; some Mu'tazila said triangle; most said square; the correct view according to the text is cube.
The cube view is based on six sides.
3
Understand the reasoning for cube
The reasoning: they affirmed six sides, and six atoms can attach from six sides, therefore the shape must be cubical.
This shows the connection between shape and how atoms combine.

This is the recording of the possible doctrines in this subject. And let us now recall the subdivisions of each of these divisions. As for the subdivisions of the first division: it is the statement of those who affirm the part that is indivisible. They are matters: The first branch: They disagreed about whether it is conceivable that a single part can be placed contiguous to two parts? Al-Jubba'i and al-Ash'ari rejected it, while Abu Hashim and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar permitted it. The second branch: Whether the individual atom (al-jawhar al-fard) has a shape? Al-Ash'ari denied it, but most of the Mu'tazila affirmed a shape for it. Then they disagreed. Some of them said it resembles a triangle, while most said it resembles a square. The truth is that they likened it to a cube, because they affirmed for it six sides. And they claimed that six atoms can be attached to it from six sides. All denied it except Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi, from the early Mu'tazila. He claimed that it must necessarily possess a certain amount of length, width, and depth. The fourth branch: Whether the individual atom is receptive to life (hayat) and other accidents conditional upon life, such as knowledge, will, and power? Al-Ash'ari and a group of early Mu'tazila affirmed it, while the later Mu'tazila denied it. And this is the well-known issue concerning the fourth branch.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents four branches (furu') of the first view (the atomist position that the body is composed of indivisible parts). The author first introduces the subdivisions of this view. The first branch concerns whether an indivisible atom can be placed contiguous to two other atoms simultaneously. Al-Jubba'i and al-Ash'ari denied this possibility, while Abu Hashim and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar permitted it. The second branch asks whether the individual atom has a shape. Al-Ash'ari denied it has any shape, but most Mu'tazila affirmed it and debated the specific shape: some said triangle, most said square, but the correct view according to them is a cube, because they held that an atom has six sides, allowing six atoms to attach from six directions. The third branch asks whether the atom has any share of length and width. All denied it except Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi, who insisted that an atom must have some dimension in length, width, and depth. The author notes this as the well-known issue concerning the fourth branch.

?

Will you commit to seeking the 'why' behind your beliefs from this day forward?
?
Al-Ash'ari denied the atom has any shape, perhaps to avoid attributing physical properties to the unseen. Is there an area of your faith where you avoid thinking deeply about the details because you fear uncertainty? Are you willing to trust that seeking understanding, like the Mu'tazila did, can strengthen your faith rather than weaken it?

Do Atoms Have Dimensions?
Third branch: Do atoms have length and width?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on whether individual atoms possess length, width, and depth.

Core Idea
Do atoms have dimensions? Almost all said no, except one.

The third branch asks whether the individual atom has any share of length and width (and depth). Nearly all scholars denied it, except Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi from the early Mu'tazila, who insisted that an atom must have some amount of length, width, and depth.

Guided Path
1
Read the third branch passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the near-unanimous denial and the lone exception.
This is a straightforward disagreement.
2
Identify the majority and minority positions
The majority denied that atoms have dimensions; only Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi affirmed dimensions.
He is from the early Mu'tazila.
3
Consider the implications
If atoms have no dimensions, how can they combine to form extended bodies? This was a key issue.
Think about the problem of composition from dimensionless parts.

This is the recording of the possible doctrines in this subject. And let us now recall the subdivisions of each of these divisions. As for the subdivisions of the first division: it is the statement of those who affirm the part that is indivisible. They are matters: The first branch: They disagreed about whether it is conceivable that a single part can be placed contiguous to two parts? Al-Jubba'i and al-Ash'ari rejected it, while Abu Hashim and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar permitted it. The second branch: Whether the individual atom (al-jawhar al-fard) has a shape? Al-Ash'ari denied it, but most of the Mu'tazila affirmed a shape for it. Then they disagreed. Some of them said it resembles a triangle, while most said it resembles a square. Can Atoms Receive Life-Accidents?
Fourth branch: Can atoms receive life-accidents?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement over whether atoms can receive life and life-contingent accidents like knowledge, will, and power.

Core Idea
Can atoms be alive? Early scholars said yes, later Mu'tazila said no.

The fourth branch addresses whether the individual atom is receptive to life (hayat) and other accidents that depend on life, such as knowledge, will, and power. Al-Ash'ari and early Mu'tazila affirmed this possibility, but later Mu'tazila denied it. This issue became well-known as the 'fourth branch' issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth branch passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two camps: early scholars (including al-Ash'ari) affirmed, later Mu'tazila denied.
The author notes this is a well-known issue.
2
Identify the positions
Al-Ash'ari and early Mu'tazila said yes, later Mu'tazila said no.
The later Mu'tazila represent a shift in doctrine.
3
Reflect on the significance
If atoms can receive life-accidents, then life is not exclusive to composite bodies; it can inhere in a single atom. This has implications for the nature of life and its relation to body composition.
This connects to broader theological issues about the soul and life.

The First Branch: Can a Single Atom Occupy Two Points?
Al-Razi presents the first branch: can one atom occupy two contiguous points?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement among theologians about whether a single indivisible atom can simultaneously occupy two contiguous points.

Core Idea
Can one atom be in two places at once? Theologians split.

This lesson begins our exploration of the six branches of debate within the view that affirms the indivisible atom. The first branch addresses a fundamental question: can a single atom occupy a continuum of two points? Al-Ash'ari and al-Jubba'i deny it, while Abu Hashim and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar allow it. Understanding this issue is key to grasping how atoms interact in space.

Guided Path
1
Read the branch text
Read the Arabic and translation for the first branch. Note the names of the scholars on each side.
The question is about 'occurring upon a continuum of two parts' – i.e., occupying two contiguous points simultaneously.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify who denied it (al-Jubba'i and al-Ash'ari) and who allowed it (Abu Hashim and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar).
This is a clear split between early Ash'ari and Mu'tazili figures.
3
Reflect on the implication
Think about why this matters for a theory of atoms: if an atom cannot occupy two points, then atoms must be point-like and cannot overlap.
This underpins later discussions about shape and contact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا ضبط المذاهب الممكنة في هذا الباب ولتذكر الآن تفاريع كل واحد من هذه الأقسام : أما تفريعات القسم الأول : وهو قول المثبتين للجزء الذي لا يتجزأ فهي أشياء : الفرع الأول : اختلفوا في أنه هل يعقل وقوع الجزء الواحد على متصل الجزءين ؟ فأباه الجبائي والأشعري وجوزه أبو هاشم والقاضي عبد الجبار . الفرع الثاني : إن الجوهر الفرد هل له شكل أم لا ؟ فأباه الأشعري وأما أكثر المعتزلة فقد أثبتوا له شكلا ثم اختلفوا فمنهم من قال إنه أشبه بالمثلث والأكثرون قالوا إنه أشبه بالمربع والحق أنهم شبهوه بالمكعب لأنهم أثبتوا له جوانب ستة وزعموا أنه يمكن أن تحصل به جواهر ستة من جوانب ستة وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يجب أن يكون شكله بالمكعب . الفرع الثالث : إن الجوهر الواحد هل له حظ من الأطوال والعروض ؟ فالكل أنكروه إلا أبا الحسين الصالحي من قدماء المعتزلة فإنه زعم أنه لا بد وأن يحصل له قدر من الطول والعرض والعمق . الفرع الرابع : إن الجوهر الفرد هل يقبل الحياة وسائر الأعراض المشروطة بالحياة كالعلم والإرادة والقدرة ؟ الأشعري وجماعة من قدماء المعتزلة قالوا به والمتأخرون من المعتزلة أنكروه وهذه هي المسألة المشهورة في علم الكلام بأن البنية هل هي شرط للحياة وللأعراض المشروطة بالحياة أم ؟ الفرع الخامس : إن الخط المؤلف من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ هل يمكن أن يجعل دائرة أم لا ؟ أما الأشعري فقد أنكره في كتاب النوادر وذهب إمام الحرمين في الشامل إلى أنه جائز . الفرع السادس : إن كل من أثبت الجوهر الفرد فإنه زعم أن حجر الرحى يتفكك عند الاستدارة .
This is the recording of the possible schools of thought in this chapter. Now we mention the subdivisions of each of these sections: As for the subdivisions of the first section, which is the statement of those who affirm the part that is indivisible, they are things: The first branch: They differed on whether it is conceivable for a single part to occur upon a continuum of two parts? Al-Jubba'i and al-Ash'ari denied it, and Abu Hashim and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar allowed it. The second branch: Does the individual atom have a shape or not? Al-Ash'ari denied it, but most of the Mu'tazila affirmed a shape for it. Then they differed: some said it resembles a triangle, and most said it resembles a square. The truth is that they likened it to a cube because they affirmed six sides for it and claimed that six atoms can be obtained from six sides. On this assumption, its shape must be cubic. The third branch: Does the single atom have a share of length and width? All denied it except Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi among the early Mu'tazila, who claimed that it must have a measure of length, width, and depth. The fourth branch: Does the individual atom accept life and the other accidents conditioned by life, such as knowledge, will, and power? Al-Ash'ari and a group of early Mu'tazila affirmed it, but the later Mu'tazila denied it. This is the famous issue in kalam: whether bodily structure is a condition for life and for the accidents conditioned by life or not? The fifth branch: Can the line composed of indivisible parts be made a circle or not? Al-Ash'ari denied it in his book al-Nawadir, and Imam al-Haramayn in al-Shamil held that it is permissible. The sixth branch: Everyone who affirms the individual atom claimed that millstones disintegrate upon rotation.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of six subdivisions that arise from the position affirming the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazzā). The author first introduces the chapter and then states that he will list the subdivisions of each section. For the first section, he lists six branches. The first branch concerns whether a single atom can simultaneously occupy two contiguous points; some theologians deny it while others allow it. The second branch debates the shape of the atom: al-Ash'ari denies it, but most Mu'tazila affirm a shape, with disagreements over whether it is triangular, square, or cubic. The third branch asks whether the atom has dimensions; this is denied by all except one early Mu'tazili. The fourth branch asks whether the atom can accept life and life-conditioned accidents; some affirm and others deny, and this is linked to the famous issue of whether bodily structure is a condition for life. The fifth branch considers whether a line of atoms can form a circle; al-Ash'ari denies it, but Imam al-Haramayn allows it. The sixth branch states that all who affirm the atom claim that millstones disintegrate during rotation—a necessary consequence of their view. Each branch represents a distinct locus of debate within kalām physics.

متصل الجزءين

Correction: Even among those who affirm indivisible atoms, there is significant dispute over their properties, such as whether they can occupy two points.

Key Takeaway
The first branch reveals a fundamental split in atomist theology: some deny a single atom can occupy two contiguous points, while others affirm it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that al-Jubba'i and al-Ash'ari denied it but Abu Hashim and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar allowed it, which side did you instinctively lean toward? What does that instinct reveal about how you usually decide what is possible for Allah's creation?
?
This debate forces you to ask: 'What do I assume about the nature of physical objects?' Have you ever stopped to examine whether your assumptions about reality come from the Qur'an and Sunnah, or just from your own limited reasoning? Will you pause today to ask Allah to make your beliefs about His creation based on His revelation, not your instincts?
?
The question here is about rational conceivability—'ya'qilu'. Can you think of a belief you hold about Allah or the unseen that you've never actually examined because it seemed 'obviously true'? What would it take for you to seriously consider the other side of that belief?

The Second Branch: Does the Atom Have a Shape?
Al-Razi presents the second branch: does the atom have a shape?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether the individual atom has a shape, and the positions on what shape it might be.

Core Idea
Atoms: shapeless or cubic? Theologians disagree.

The second branch moves from spatial occupancy to shape. Does the indivisible atom have a form? Al-Ash'ari denies it entirely, while most Mu'tazila affirm a shape, with disagreements over whether it resembles a triangle, square, or cube. This lesson examines the arguments and the reasoning behind the cubic conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the branch text
Read the Arabic and translation for the second branch. Note the different positions on shape.
Al-Ash'ari denies shape; Mu'tazila affirm shape but differ on details.
2
Understand the cubic reasoning
The text says the 'truth' is that they likened it to a cube because they affirmed six sides and claimed that six atoms can be arranged from six sides.
This implies atoms must be cubic to allow contact on all sides.
3
Reflect on the implications
If atoms have shape, they have extension and boundaries, affecting how they combine to form bodies.
Shape is crucial for explaining physical interactions like contact and arrangement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا ضبط المذاهب الممكنة في هذا الباب ولتذكر الآن تفاريع كل واحد من هذه الأقسام : أما تفريعات القسم الأول : وهو قول المثبتين للجزء الذي لا يتجزأ فهي أشياء : الفرع الأول : اختلفوا في أنه هل يعقل وقوع الجزء الواحد على متصل الجزءين ؟ فأباه الجبائي والأشعري وجوزه أبو هاشم والقاضي عبد الجبار . الفرع الثاني : إن الجوهر الفرد هل له شكل أم لا ؟ فأباه الأشعري وأما أكثر المعتزلة فقد أثبتوا له شكلا ثم اختلفوا فمنهم من قال إنه أشبه بالمثلث والأكثرون قالوا إنه أشبه بالمربع والحق أنهم شبهوه بالمكعب لأنهم أثبتوا له جوانب ستة وزعموا أنه يمكن أن تحصل به جواهر ستة من جوانب ستة وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يجب أن يكون شكله بالمكعب . الفرع الثالث : إن الجوهر الواحد هل له حظ من الأطوال والعروض ؟ فالكل أنكروه إلا أبا الحسين الصالحي من قدماء المعتزلة فإنه زعم أنه لا بد وأن يحصل له قدر من الطول والعرض والعمق . الفرع الرابع : إن الجوهر الفرد هل يقبل الحياة وسائر الأعراض المشروطة بالحياة كالعلم والإرادة والقدرة ؟ الأشعري وجماعة من قدماء المعتزلة قالوا به والمتأخرون من المعتزلة أنكروه وهذه هي المسألة المشهورة في علم الكلام بأن البنية هل هي شرط للحياة وللأعراض المشروطة بالحياة أم ؟ الفرع الخامس : إن الخط المؤلف من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ هل يمكن أن يجعل دائرة أم لا ؟ أما الأشعري فقد أنكره في كتاب النوادر وذهب إمام الحرمين في الشامل إلى أنه جائز . الفرع السادس : إن كل من أثبت الجوهر الفرد فإنه زعم أن حجر الرحى يتفكك عند الاستدارة .
This is the recording of the possible schools of thought in this chapter. Now we mention the subdivisions of each of these sections: As for the subdivisions of the first section, which is the statement of those who affirm the part that is indivisible, they are things: The first branch: They differed on whether it is conceivable for a single part to occur upon a continuum of two parts? Al-Jubba'i and al-Ash'ari denied it, and Abu Hashim and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar allowed it. The second branch: Does the individual atom have a shape or not? Al-Ash'ari denied it, but most of the Mu'tazila affirmed a shape for it. Then they differed: some said it resembles a triangle, and most said it resembles a square. The truth is that they likened it to a cube because they affirmed six sides for it and claimed that six atoms can be obtained from six sides. On this assumption, its shape must be cubic. The third branch: Does the single atom have a share of length and width? All denied it except Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi among the early Mu'tazila, who claimed that it must have a measure of length, width, and depth. The fourth branch: Does the individual atom accept life and the other accidents conditioned by life, such as knowledge, will, and power? Al-Ash'ari and a group of early Mu'tazila affirmed it, but the later Mu'tazila denied it. This is the famous issue in kalam: whether bodily structure is a condition for life and for the accidents conditioned by life or not? The fifth branch: Can the line composed of indivisible parts be made a circle or not? Al-Ash'ari denied it in his book al-Nawadir, and Imam al-Haramayn in al-Shamil held that it is permissible. The sixth branch: Everyone who affirms the individual atom claimed that millstones disintegrate upon rotation.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of six subdivisions that arise from the position affirming the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazzā). The author first introduces the chapter and then states that he will list the subdivisions of each section. For the first section, he lists six branches. The first branch concerns whether a single atom can simultaneously occupy two contiguous points; some theologians deny it while others allow it. The second branch debates the shape of the atom: al-Ash'ari denies it, but most Mu'tazila affirm a shape, with disagreements over whether it is triangular, square, or cubic. The third branch asks whether the atom has dimensions; this is denied by all except one early Mu'tazili. The fourth branch asks whether the atom can accept life and life-conditioned accidents; some affirm and others deny, and this is linked to the famous issue of whether bodily structure is a condition for life. The fifth branch considers whether a line of atoms can form a circle; al-Ash'ari denies it, but Imam al-Haramayn allows it. The sixth branch states that all who affirm the atom claim that millstones disintegrate during rotation—a necessary consequence of their view. Each branch represents a distinct locus of debate within kalām physics.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
شكل
مثلث
مربع
مكعب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Ash'ari also affirmed a shape, but he denied it.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari explicitly denied that atoms have any shape, while Mu'tazila affirmed it.

Checkpoint
What shape did the author (Al-Razi) claim is the 'truth' for the atom?
A) Triangle
B) Square
C) Cube
D) Sphere

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari denied atomic shape; most Mu'tazila affirmed it, with the cubic shape being the most consistent with six-sided contact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read a debate about the shape of something no one has ever seen—the atom. How does this challenge or affirm your own approach to accepting theological truths that are not empirically verified? Will you rely more on reasoned deduction or on reported revelation for matters of the unseen?
?
This passage shows scholars using logical reasoning to infer properties of the unseen. Reflect on a time you relied on reason rather than direct observation to reach a conclusion about your faith. Were you as rigorous as these scholars in considering multiple possibilities? Commit to one area of your faith where you will seek stronger rational grounding.
?
Al-Ash'ari denied atomic shape while others affirmed. How comfortable are you with disagreement among respected scholars on matters of Islamic theology? Does it trouble you, or does it encourage you to study more deeply? This week, look into a theological disagreement you have ignored and try to understand both sides.

The Third Branch: Does the Atom Have Dimensions?
Al-Razi presents the third branch: does the atom have dimensions?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute over whether the indivisible atom has any measure of length, width, or depth.

Core Idea
Atoms: dimensionless points or tiny cubes? One lone scholar says they have size.

The third branch asks whether the atom has 'share' of length, width, and depth. The majority answer is no, but one early Mu'tazili, Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi, insisted that atoms must have some dimensions. This lesson explores the implications of this rare view.

Guided Path
1
Read the branch text
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the near-unanimous denial and the lone dissenter.
Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi is an early Mu'tazili figure.
2
Contrast with the second branch
The second branch affirmed shape; this branch denies dimensions. Consider how shape without dimensions is possible.
This reveals tensions within atomist theories.
3
Reflect on the minority view
If atoms have length, width, and depth, they become extended and potentially divisible. Why might al-Salihi insist on this?
He likely saw dimensions as necessary for atoms to form bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a creedal issue where almost all scholars agree except one, do you tend to follow the majority or investigate the lone opinion? What does that choice reveal about your trust in scholarly authority—and are you willing to defend that position before Allah?
?
This lesson shows that a single early scholar, al-Salihi, held a view on atomic dimensions that was rejected by everyone else. Do you hold any belief (e.g., about the nature of the universe, God, or the soul) that you have never critically examined because 'everyone says so'? How would you test that belief against revelation and reason?
?
The atom is an unseen entity that scholars debated based on logical necessity. If you were to explain to a skeptic why rational inquiry into the unseen world is valid, how would you use this passage as a model? Are you personally convinced that your own conclusions about the unseen are built on the same careful reasoning?

The Fourth Branch: Can the Atom Accept Life?
Al-Razi presents the fourth branch: can the atom accept life?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether the indivisible atom can be a locus for life and its associated accidents, such as knowledge and power.

Core Idea
Can an atom be alive? This branch links atomism to the condition of bodily structure for life.

The fourth branch moves from physical to metaphysical properties: can an individual atom accept life and the accidents conditioned by life, like knowledge, will, and power? Al-Ash'ari and early Mu'tazila said yes, but later Mu'tazila denied it. This is connected to the famous kalam issue of whether bodily structure (al-bunya) is a condition for life.

Guided Path
1
Read the branch text
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two camps: al-Ash'ari + early Mu'tazila vs. later Mu'tazila.
The text explicitly links this to the famous issue of 'al-bunya' (bodily structure).
2
Understand the link to bodily structure
The question is whether life can exist in a single atom or requires a composite body. Al-Ash'ari and early Mu'tazila allow life in an atom, implying structure is not necessary.
Later Mu'tazila insisted that life only appears in a body with structure (al-bunya).
3
Reflect on the theological stakes
If an atom can be alive, then God could create life in a single atom. This relates to divine omnipotence.
This branch has implications for the nature of life itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If bodily structure is a condition for life, how does that change your view of your own body? Are you treating it as a mere container or as an essential condition for your existence and worship? What change do you need to make in how you care for it?
?
The later Mu'tazila required structure for life to exist. Do you ever act as if your faith is 'atomic'—just a simple declaration without structure and practice? What structure (e.g., prayer, charity, community) is missing that would make your faith truly alive?
?
Al-Ash'ari affirmed that God can give life even to a single atom. How does knowing that God can bring life to the smallest particle affect your trust in Him to give you spiritual life when you feel spiritually dead? What step will you take to seek that life?

The Fifth Branch: Can an Atomic Line Form a Circle?
Al-Razi presents the fifth branch: can an atomic line be made a circle?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement over whether a line composed of indivisible atoms can be bent into a circle.

Core Idea
Can atomic 'Lego' make a perfect circle? Al-Ash'ari says no, but others disagree.

The fifth branch tackles geometry: if a line is made of discrete indivisible atoms, can it be arranged to form a circle? Al-Ash'ari denied this in his book al-Nawadir, but Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni in al-Shamil allowed it. This branch tests the limits of atomistic geometry.

Guided Path
1
Read the branch text
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two opposing views.
The text references specific works: al-Nawadir and al-Shamil.
2
Consider the geometric problem
A circle requires a continuous curve. If the line is made of discrete atoms, can it approximate a circle? Al-Ash'ari thought not.
Imam al-Haramayn argued it is permissible (ja'iz).
3
Reflect on mathematical vs. physical possibility
This is a debate about what is rationally conceivable given atomism.
It connects to broader issues of continuity and discreteness.

هذا ضبط المذاهب الممكنة في هذا الباب ولتذكر الآن تفاريع كل واحد من هذه الأقسام : أما تفريعات القسم الأول : وهو قول المثبتين للجزء الذي لا يتجزأ فهي أشياء : الفرع الأول : اختلفوا في أنه هل يعقل وقوع الجزء الواحد على متصل الجزءين ؟ فأباه الجبائي والأشعري وجوزه أبو هاشم والقاضي عبد الجبار . الفرع الثاني : إن الجوهر الفرد هل له شكل أم لا ؟ فأباه الأشعري وأما أكثر المعتزلة فقد أثبتوا له شكلا ثم اختلفوا فمنهم من قال إنه أشبه بالمثلث والأكثرون قالوا إنه أشبه بالمربع والحق أنهم شبهوه بالمكعب لأنهم أثبتوا له جوانب ستة وزعموا أنه يمكن أن تحصل به جواهر ستة من جوانب ستة وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يجب أن يكون شكله بالمكعب . الفرع الثالث : إن الجوهر الواحد هل له حظ من الأطوال والعروض ؟ فالكل أنكروه إلا أبا الحسين الصالحي من قدماء المعتزلة فإنه زعم أنه لا بد وأن يحصل له قدر من الطول والعرض والعمق . الفرع الرابع : إن الجوهر الفرد هل يقبل الحياة وسائر الأعراض المشروطة بالحياة كالعلم والإرادة والقدرة ؟ الأشعري وجماعة من قدماء المعتزلة قالوا به والمتأخرون من المعتزلة أنكروه وهذه هي المسألة المشهورة في علم الكلام بأن البنية هل هي شرط للحياة وللأعراض المشروطة بالحياة أم ؟ الفرع الخامس : إن الخط المؤلف من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ هل يمكن أن يجعل دائرة أم لا ؟ أما الأشعري فقد أنكره في كتاب النوادر وذهب إمام الحرمين في الشامل إلى أنه جائز . الفرع السادس : إن كل من أثبت الجوهر الفرد فإنه زعم أن حجر الرحى يتفكك عند الاستدارة .
This is the recording of the possible schools of thought in this chapter. Now we mention the subdivisions of each of these sections: As for the subdivisions of the first section, which is the statement of those who affirm the part that is indivisible, they are things: The first branch: They differed on whether it is conceivable for a single part to occur upon a continuum of two parts? Al-Jubba'i and al-Ash'ari denied it, and Abu Hashim and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar allowed it. The second branch: Does the individual atom have a shape or not? Al-Ash'ari denied it, but most of the Mu'tazila affirmed a shape for it. Then they differed: some said it resembles a triangle, and most said it resembles a square. The truth is that they likened it to a cube because they affirmed six sides for it and claimed that six atoms can be obtained from six sides. On this assumption, its shape must be cubic. The third branch: Does the single atom have a share of length and width? All denied it except Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi among the early Mu'tazila, who claimed that it must have a measure of length, width, and depth. The fourth branch: Does the individual atom accept life and the other accidents conditioned by life, such as knowledge, will, and power? Al-Ash'ari and a group of early Mu'tazila affirmed it, but the later Mu'tazila denied it. This is the famous issue in kalam: whether bodily structure is a condition for life and for the accidents conditioned by life or not? The fifth branch: Can the line composed of indivisible parts be made a circle or not? Al-Ash'ari denied it in his book al-Nawadir, and Imam al-Haramayn in al-Shamil held that it is permissible. The sixth branch: Everyone who affirms the individual atom claimed that millstones disintegrate upon rotation.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of six subdivisions that arise from the position affirming the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazzā). The author first introduces the chapter and then states that he will list the subdivisions of each section. For the first section, he lists six branches. The first branch concerns whether a single atom can simultaneously occupy two contiguous points; some theologians deny it while others allow it. The second branch debates the shape of the atom: al-Ash'ari denies it, but most Mu'tazila affirm a shape, with disagreements over whether it is triangular, square, or cubic. The third branch asks whether the atom has dimensions; this is denied by all except one early Mu'tazili. The fourth branch asks whether the atom can accept life and life-conditioned accidents; some affirm and others deny, and this is linked to the famous issue of whether bodily structure is a condition for life. The fifth branch considers whether a line of atoms can form a circle; al-Ash'ari denies it, but Imam al-Haramayn allows it. The sixth branch states that all who affirm the atom claim that millstones disintegrate during rotation—a necessary consequence of their view. Each branch represents a distinct locus of debate within kalām physics.

دائرة

Misconception: One might think al-Ash'ari allowed circular atomic lines, but he denied it.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari explicitly denied the possibility, while Imam al-Haramayn allowed it.

Checkpoint
Who denied that a line of indivisible atoms can be made into a circle?
A) Imam al-Haramayn
B) Al-Ash'ari
C) Most Mu'tazila
D) The later Mu'tazila

Key Takeaway
The fifth branch shows divergence on whether atomistic geometry can produce a circle: al-Ash'ari denies it, Imam al-Haramayn affirms its possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are confronted with a truth that does not fit your current mental models—like a circle from discrete atoms—do you dismiss it like al-Ash'ari, or do you humbly consider its possibility like Imam al-Haramayn? Where in your life are you resisting because it feels impossible?
?
Have you ever limited Allah's creative power by your own logic, assuming something cannot be just because you cannot imagine how? Will you repent from this intellectual pride and affirm that Allah is able to do all things, even what seems contradictory to your finite mind?
?
The atomic line cannot make a perfect circle—yet Imam al-Haramayn saw it as permissible. In what area of your faith have you settled for 'impossible' when Allah says 'possible'? What step can you take today to expand your conviction in Allah's absolute power?

The Sixth Branch: Millstones Disintegrate on Rotation
Al-Razi presents the sixth branch: millstones disintegrate on rotation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consequence that all proponents of the indivisible atom affirm: millstones disintegrate when rotating.

Core Idea
If atoms exist, millstones must fall apart when they spin.

The sixth and final branch presents a necessary consequence of affirming the indivisible atom: that millstones (grinding stones) disintegrate upon rotation. This is not an empirical claim but a logical conclusion from atomist premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the branch text
Read the Arabic and translation. The claim is simple and universal: all who affirm the atom agree that millstones disintegrate.
This is a consequence, not a debated issue.
2
Understand the reasoning
If a millstone rotates, its rim moves faster than its center. If it is made of atoms, the atoms at the rim would separate due to different speeds, causing disintegration.
This argument is used to attack atomism, but here it is presented as a logical outcome.
3
Reflect on the value of this branch
This branch shows how atomists accepted counterintuitive physical consequences.
It demonstrates the coherence of their system.

The Second Position: Infinite Parts and the Leap
Al-Razi presents, objects to, and critiques the second position's defense of the leap.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why proponents of the second position (infinite parts in a finite body) were forced to introduce the concept of the leap (tafra) and why this was rejected by most rational thinkers.

Core Idea
To avoid an absurd conclusion, they invented an even more absurd one: the leap.

Al-Razi continues his critique of positions on body composition. The second position—that a finite body is composed of actually infinite parts—faces a devastating objection: traversing infinite points in finite time is impossible. To escape, its supporters invented the 'leap' (tafra), claiming a mover can skip over intermediate points. Al-Razi notes that most rational thinkers consider this obviously false, and he draws a parallel to the absurdity of the atomist position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second position
Read the passage and note the claim: a finite body is composed of actually infinite parts. This is the position being discussed.
This is the second of the divisions introduced earlier.
2
Identify the objection
The objection is that if the body has infinite parts, then moving from one end to the other would require traversing an infinite number of points in finite time, which is impossible.
3
Examine the defense: the leap
Proponents respond by saying the mover does not pass through all parts but 'leaps' over some. Note the definition of leap: transferring without passing through the space between.
This is an ad hoc solution.
4
Consider the critique
Al-Razi states that most rational thinkers consider this obviously corrupt. He draws a parallel to the millstone disintegration required by atomists, showing both positions lead to absurdities.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تفريعات القسم الثاني من الأقسام الذكورة، وهو كون الجسم المتناهي في المقدار : مركباً من أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل . فاعلم : أنه لما قيل لهم : لو كان هذا الجسم مركباً من الأجزاء ، التي لا نهاية لها بالفعل ، وجب يمتنع حصول المتحرك من أولها إلى آخرها في زمان متناهي . فعند هذا قالوا : المتحرك لم يتحرك على جميع تلك الأجزاء ، بل تحرك على بعضها ، وطفر على الباقي . وفسروا الطفر : بانتقال الجسم من مكان إلى مكان آخر ، من غير أن يمر بما بينهما . وأكثر العقلاء اتفقوا على فساد هذا القول معلوم بالضرورة . وبالجملة : فكما أن القائلين بالقول الأول ، لزمهم التزام تفكك حجر الرحى عند الاستدارة . فالقائلون بهذا القول الثاني ، التزموا حصول الطفرة وكلاهما في غاية البعد
As for the subdivisions of the second section among the mentioned sections, which is that a finite body in quantity is composed of parts infinite in actuality. Know that when it was said to them: If this body were composed of parts that are actually infinite, then it would be impossible for a mover to traverse from its beginning to its end in a finite time. At this point they said: The mover did not move over all those parts, but rather moved over some of them and leaped over the rest. And they explained 'al-tafra' as the transfer of a body from one place to another without passing through what is between them. Most rational thinkers agreed that the corruption of this statement is known by necessity. In summary: just as the proponents of the first statement were compelled to accept the disintegration of millstones upon rotation, so the proponents of this second statement were compelled to accept the occurrence of the leap—both extremely remote.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage presents the second position and its attempted defense against an obvious objection. The position holds that a finite body is actually composed of an infinite number of parts. The objection is: if so, crossing the body would require traversing an infinite number of positions in finite time, which is impossible. To avoid this, supporters introduce the concept of 'tafra' (leap): the moving body does not pass through every intermediate point but rather jumps over some. They define the leap as moving from one place to another without passing through the space between. The author then states that most rational thinkers (al-ʿuqalāʾ) deem this obviously false. He concludes by drawing a parallel: just as atomists must accept that millstones disintegrate during rotation (an absurdity), so advocates of infinite parts must accept the leap—both are extremely far-fetched. The passage is a critique of both positions by showing their respective unacceptable consequences.

الجسم المتناهي
أجزاء غير متناهية
المتحرك

Correction: The passage presents the leap as an ad hoc response to a logical objection, and it is rejected by most rational thinkers as obviously false.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Can you think of a specific example in your own life where you did this? This week, when someone challenges your view, pause and ask yourself: 'Am I accepting an absurdity to avoid changing my mind?'
?
Al-Razi says the leap is 'known by necessity' to be false. Is there any belief you hold that, when examined honestly, you know in your heart to be false, but you cling to it out of habit or comfort? What would it look like to surrender that belief to the truth?
?
The proponents of the leap tried to salvage their theory by saying the mover 'skips over' parts. In your spiritual life, are there areas where you skip over uncomfortable truths – about your character, your relationships, or your obedience to Allah – rather than facing them head-on? Philosophers on Simple Body and Separation
Al-Razi introduces the philosophers' view and their redefinition of separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' definition of a simple body and their unconventional redefinition of separation as producing multiplicity.

Core Idea
Separation is not about distance, but about creating multiplicity.

In this passage, Al-Razi begins to present the view of the philosophers regarding the nature of bodies. They argue that a simple body is a single entity both in itself and in sensation. Crucially, they redefine the concept of separation (tafrīq) in a way that counters the physical understanding of distancing parts. Al-Razi immediately flags this as extremely far-fetched and begins a refutation, though the argument is cut short.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and then the literal translation. Notice the flow: first, the philosophers' agreement on the simple body, then their definition of separation, and finally the author's critical remark.
The passage is incomplete; it cuts off at 'if...'
2
Understand the claim about the simple body
The philosophers assert that a simple body is one entity in itself, just as it appears to the senses. This is their starting point.
3
Grasp the redefinition of separation
They explicitly reject the common understanding of separation as physical distancing. Instead, they define it as 'producing multiplicity' (iḥdāth al-taʿaddud). This is a conceptual, not physical, operation.
This redefinition is key to their system.
4
Note the author's criticism
Al-Razi immediately labels this view as extremely far-fetched and begins a rebuttal with 'for we say: if...'. The manuscript cuts off here, but the criticism is clearly stated.
Observe that the author does not accept this redefinition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تفريعات القسم الثالث وهو قول الفلاسفة : فاعلم : أنهم اتفقوا على أن الجسم البسيط شيء واحد في نفسه كما أنه في الحس شيء واحد وزعموا : أن التفريق ليس عبارة عن تبعيد المتجاورين ، وتفريق المتماسين . بل هو عبارة عن : إحداث التعدد وهذا أيضاً في غاية البعد . لأنا نقول : إذا
As for the subdivisions of the third section, which is the statement of the philosophers: Know that they agreed that the simple body is one entity in itself just as it is one entity in sensation. And they claimed that separation (al-tafrīq) is not an expression of distancing what is adjacent and separating what is in contact, but rather it is an expression of producing multiplicity. This is also extremely far-fetched. For we say: if...
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage introduces the philosophers' position on the nature of bodies. The author states that they agree the simple body is a single thing in itself, as it appears to the senses. They then define 'separation' (tafrīq) not as the physical distancing of contiguous parts, but as the production of multiplicity (iḥdāth al-taʿaddud). The author immediately criticizes this as extremely far-fetched (fī ghāyat al-buʿd) and begins a refutation that is cut off: 'for we say: if...' The passage is incomplete in the manuscript, but it clearly sets up the philosophers' counterintuitive definition and the author's critical response.

Fifth and Sixth Branches on Atoms and Composition
Razi presents two branches of atomic theory: the circle and the millstone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that atomism, in the eyes of its critics, leads to problematic consequences such as the impossibility of a circular line composed of indivisible parts and the physical paradox of a millstone disintegrating upon rotation.

Core Idea
Atomic theory implies paradoxical physical consequences.

Razi presents two specific branches of consequences that follow from the theory of indivisible atoms (jawhar fard). These are not merely abstract; they challenge the coherence of atomism in geometry and everyday physics. As you read, consider why these consequences were seen as problematic by scholars like al-Ash'ari and al-Juwayni.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the branches
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the two branches: the first about forming a circle, the second about the millstone. Pay attention to the scholars' positions.
2
Analyze the first branch
Consider why al-Ash'ari denied that a line of indivisible atoms could form a circle, while al-Juwayni thought it possible. Think about the geometric implications.
3
Reflect on the millstone paradox
Understand why affirming the indivisible atom leads to the claim that a millstone disintegrates when rotating. This highlights a physical absurdity that critics of atomism used.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الخامس : إن الخط المؤلف من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ . هل يمكن أن يجعل دائرة أم لا ؟ أما الأشعري ، فقد أنكره في كتاب « النوادر » وذهب : إمام الحرمين ، في « الشامل » إلى أنه جائز . والفرع السادس : إن كل من أثبت الجوهر الفرد ، فإنه زعم : أن حجر الرحى يتفكك عند الاستدارة .
The fifth branch: Is it possible for a line composed of indivisible parts to form a circle or not? As for al-Ash'ari, he denied it in the book 'al-Nawadir'. And Imam al-Haramayn [al-Juwayni] in 'al-Shamil' held that it is possible. And the sixth branch: Every one who affirms the individual atom (jawhar fard) claims that the millstone disintegrates upon rotation.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage presents two branches (furuʿ) under the first opinion regarding atomic composition. The first branch asks whether a line made of indivisible atoms can form a circle. Al-Ash'ari denied this, while al-Juwayni considered it possible. The second branch states that anyone who affirms the indivisible atom must accept that the millstone disintegrates when it rotates, because the atoms would need to separate. These are presented as consequences of the atomic theory that are difficult to accept.

The Leap (Ṭafra): A Failed Solution to Infinite Parts
Razi discusses the leap (ṭafra) as a response to the infinity problem and dismisses it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of 'leaping' (ṭafra) fails to resolve the objection against actually infinite parts.

Core Idea
Leaping does not solve the problem; it is merely a cover for absurdity.

When faced with the objection that a mover cannot traverse actually infinite parts in finite time, proponents of infinite parts proposed the 'leap' (ṭafra). In this lesson, we examine this response and the rebuttal by Razi and the majority of rationalists. The passage reveals why the leap is considered an obviously false and far-fetched notion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation to get an overview of the argument: objection, response, and refutation.
Pay attention to how the objection is framed and the proponents' counter-response.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection that leads to the leap: if a finite body has actually infinite parts, a mover cannot traverse them in finite time.
3
Understand the leap
Note the proponents' definition of 'leap' as moving directly from one place to another without passing through intermediate points.
This is an attempt to bypass the need to traverse all parts.
4
Grasp the refutation
See why the majority of rationalists reject the leap as evidently false. Razi compares it to the absurdity of the millstone disintegration from the atomic view.
Both positions are deemed extremely improbable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تفريعات القسم الثاني من الأقسام المذكورة، وهو كون الجسم المتناهي في المقدار : مركباً من أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل . فاعلم : أنه لما قيل لهم : لو كان هذا الجسم مركباً من الأجزاء ، التي لا نهاية لها بالفعل ، وجب يمتنع حصول المتحرك من أولها إلى آخرها في زمان متناهي . فعند هذا قالوا : المتحرك لم يتحرك على جميع تلك الأجزاء ، بل تحرك على بعضها ، وطفر على الباقي . وفسروا الطفر : بانتقال الجسم من مكان إلى مكان آخر ، من غير أن يمر بما بينهما . وأكثر العقلاء اتفقوا على [ان] فساد هذا القول معلوم بالضرورة . وبالجملة : فكما أن القائلين بالقول الأول ، لزمهم التزام تفكك حجر الرحى عند الاستدارة . فالقائلون بهذا القول الثاني ، التزموا حصول الطفرة وكلاهما في غاية البعد
As for the branches of the second division of the mentioned divisions, namely that the body finite in magnitude is composed of actually infinite parts. Then know: When it was said to them: If this body were composed of actually infinite parts, then it would be impossible for the mover to reach from its beginning to its end in a finite time. So upon this they said: The mover did not move over all those parts, but rather moved over some of them and leaped (ṭafara) over the rest. And they explained the leap (ṭafra) as: The transfer of a body from one place to another without passing through what is between them. The majority of rationalists agreed that the corruption of this statement is known by necessity. In sum: Just as the proponents of the first opinion were compelled to accept the disintegration of the millstone upon rotation, so the proponents of this second opinion accepted the occurrence of the leap, and both are extremely far-fetched.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage discusses the branches of the second opinion, which claims that a finite body is composed of actually infinite parts. The objection is raised that if this were so, a mover could not traverse the infinite parts in finite time. In response, proponents introduced the concept of 'leaping' (ṭafra), where the body jumps from one point to the other without passing through intermediate points. The author then notes that the majority of rationalists consider this notion evidently false. He concludes by comparing the absurdity of the millstone disintegration (from the atomic view) with the absurdity of the leap, stating both are extremely improbable.

Key Terms
الجسم المتناهي
أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل
الطفرة
متحرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The leap (ṭafra) is a valid and plausible solution to the problem of traversing infinite parts.
Correction: The leap is considered evidently false by the majority of rationalists, and is deemed extremely far-fetched.

Checkpoint

A) The mover does not need to traverse all parts.
B) The mover leaps over some parts.
C) The mover moves infinitely fast.
D) The body is actually not composed of infinite parts.

Key Takeaway
The concept of leaping (ṭafra) fails to salvage the view of actually infinite parts; it is rejected as obviously false and far-fetched.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson shows that such leaps are considered obviously false by the rationalists. Will you commit to seeking answers that hold up under honest scrutiny, even if it takes longer?
?
The proponents of the leap were already committed to the view of infinite parts, so they proposed a far-fetched solution to save their position. Are you attached to any belief or opinion so strongly that you might embrace a weak argument rather than reconsider? Can you name one such area in your life and ask God for the courage to let go of false certainty?
?
Razi describes both the leap and the millstone disintegration as 'extremely far-fetched.' When you encounter ideas that seem absurd, do you have the intellectual humility to reject them, even if they come from respected sources? Or do you sometimes cling to them out of loyalty? Let this lesson remind you that truth is not served by defending the indefensible.

Two Types of Bodily Division
Razi classifies two types of division and states consensus on estimative division's infinitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of bodily division in Islamic philosophy: estimative division (infinite by consensus) and actual division (disputed).

Core Idea
Mental division can go on forever; actual division may be finite.

Razi begins by distinguishing two ways a body can be divided: in the mind and in reality. This distinction is crucial for debates about atomism and the infinite. The philosophers agree that mental division never ends, but they differ on whether actual cutting can reach a limit.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic once, then the English. Notice that Razi first states the classification, then gives the consensus on the first type, then the disagreement on the second.
2
Identify the two types
Find the two categories: division by estimation/supposition, and division by cutting/separation. Note the key Arabic terms.
3
Understand the consensus and the dispute
Reflect on why the philosophers agree that mental division is infinite: because no matter how small, you can still imagine two sides. Then see that they differ on actual division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الانقسامات الحاصلة بحسب الوهم، وبحسب الفرض، فالفلاسفة اتفقوا على أنها غير متناهية. وذلك لأن الجسم لا ينتهي في الصغر إلى حد، إلا ويتميز أحد جانبيه عن الآخر. وأما النوع الثاني: وهو الانقسامات الحاصلة بسبب القطع والافتراق، فالفلاسفة قد اختلفوا في أنها متناهية أو غير متناهية.
The divisions occurring according to estimation and according to supposition: the philosophers agreed that they are infinite. That is because a body does not reach a limit in smallness except that one of its sides becomes distinct from the other. As for the second type: it is the divisions occurring due to cutting and separation; the philosophers differed as to whether they are finite or infinite.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a classification. It begins by stating the first type of division: divisions that occur 'according to estimation' (bi-ḥasab al-wahm) and 'according to supposition' (bi-ḥasab al-farḍ). These are mental divisions, not actual. The philosophers are unanimous that such divisions are infinite: a body can always be conceived as divided into smaller parts, because no matter how small it is, you can still distinguish one side from another. The second type is division that occurs through actual cutting and separation (al-qaṭʿ wa al-iftirāq). Here the philosophers disagree: some hold that this type is also infinite, others that it is finite. The passage sets up the two categories and then proceeds to elaborate the disputed views in what follows.

Key Terms
الانقسام بحسب الوهم
الانقسام بحسب الفرض
الانقسام بحسب القطع والافتراق
غير متناهية
متناهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'infinite' here means actual infinite parts exist in the body.
Correction: Rather it means the process of division can be repeated without end in thought, not that there are actual infinite parts.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, which type of division is unanimously agreed to be infinite?
A) Division by cutting and separation
B) Division by estimation and supposition
C) Both types are infinite
D) Neither type is infinite

Key Takeaway
The philosophers unanimously held that mental division of a body is infinite, but they disagreed on whether physical division could also be infinite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The philosophers agreed that mental division is infinite, but on actual cutting they disagreed. Is there a relationship, habit, or sin you keep analyzing in your mind but never actually sever? What would it cost you to truly separate from it?
?
If you can always imagine a smaller part, why do you hesitate to act on the part of your deen that you know needs immediate attention? What mental division are you using to postpone a real change?

Defining Density as Impenetrability
Author defines density as prevention of interior penetration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of density (kathāfa) in Islamic kalām as the property of impenetrability.

Core Idea
Density is not mass but the prevention of penetration into interiors.

In Islamic theology, the term 'density' (kathāfa) has a precise technical meaning that differs from its modern physical counterpart. The author explains that density is not about mass or weight but about a relational property that prevents one body from occupying the same space as another. This definition is foundational for discussions about bodies and spatial occupancy in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author defines density in relation to the interiors of a body.
The passage is very short, so pay close attention to each phrase.
2
Identify the two key clauses
The passage has two parts: first, a statement about penetration due to surfaces; second, the definition of density. Locate each and see how they connect.
3
Understand the logical relationship
Ask: Why does the author mention penetration before defining density? The definition is a negation of penetration—density is what prevents it.
This contrasts with common notions of density as compactness.
4
Contrast with common misconception
Many think density means 'lot of mass'. Here it is a relational property: impenetrability. Reflect on why this distinction matters for theological arguments.
This misconception is explicitly addressed in the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غوصه في البواطن ، بسبب السطوح المحيطة به . ولا معنى للكثافة إلا كـونه بحيث يمتنع كونه في البواطن .
Its penetration into interiors is due to the surfaces surrounding it. And there is no meaning of density except that it prevents it from being in the interiors.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage provides a definition of density (kathāfa). The author states that a body's penetration into interiors is caused by the surfaces surrounding it. Then he defines density: density is nothing other than the condition that prevents the body from being in interiors (i.e., impenetrability). Thus density is understood as a relational property that prevents one body from occupying the same space as another.

The Plan to Prove the Atom from Motion and Time
Author outlines his plan to prove the indivisibility of motion, time, and the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's method for proving the existence of indivisible atoms by analyzing motion and time.

Core Idea
If motion or time is indivisible, then the body must be composed of indivisible parts.

This passage opens the fourth chapter, where the author lays out his strategy for proving the indivisible atom. He will argue that motion and time are composed of discrete, indivisible units, and then deduce that the body itself must be similarly composed. He also introduces a key principle: time, motion, and distance are interdependent in terms of divisibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the author's plan.
Notice the author's confident tone as he announces his proofs.
2
Identify the two announced proofs
Find where the author states he will prove that motion consists of indivisible occurrences and that time consists of indivisible instants.
The proofs are presented in parallel structure.
3
Understand the logical entailment
See how the author connects the indivisibility of motion or time to the composition of the body from indivisible parts.
This is a conditional argument: if motion/time are indivisible, then body must be.
4
Explore the correspondence principle
Note that time, motion, and distance are said to correspond such that if one is indivisible, all are; if one is infinitely divisible, all are.
This principle is used to extend the argument.
5
Contrast with the philosophers' view
Observe how the philosophers start from infinite divisibility of body and derive infinite divisibility of motion and time.
This sets up the opposing position to be refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في الدلائل الحالة على اثبات الجوهر الفرد المبنية على اعتبار أحمال الحركة والزمان اعلم : أنا سنقيم الدلالة على أن الحركة عبارة عن حصولات متعاقبة في أحياز متلاصقة ، بحيث يكون كل واحد منها غير قابل للقسمة البتـة [ ونقيم (۱) ] الدلالة أيضاً : على أن الزمان مركب من آنات متتالية متلاصقة بحيث يكون كل واحد منها غير قابل للقسمة أصلا . ثم نبين (٢) أنه من صح هذا القول في الحركة ، أو في الزمان. فإنه يجب القطع بأن الجسم مركب من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ . واعلم : أن الزمان والحركة والمسافة ، أمور ثلاثة متطابقة . فإن ثبت في واحد منها ، كونه مركباً من أمور غير قابلة للقسمة ، ثبت في الثلاثة : كونها كذلك وإن ثبت في واحد منها كونه قابلاً للقسمة إلى غير النهاية ، ثبت في البواقي كونه كذلك (۳) . أما الفلاسفة فإنهم أثبتوا كون الجسم قابلاً لانقسامات غير متناهية . ثم فرعوا عليه كون الحركة قابلة للقسمة إلى غير النهاية . وكون الزمان قابلاً للقسمة إلى غير النهاية ، وأنه يمتنع كون الزمان مركباً من الآنات
The Fourth Chapter on the Demonstrative Proofs for the Establishment of the Indivisible Atom (al-jawhar al-fard) Based on the Consideration of the Accidents of Motion and Time. Know that we will present the proof that motion is a series of consecutive occurrences in contiguous spaces, such that each one of them is absolutely indivisible. And we will also present the proof that time is composed of successive consecutive instants (ānāt) such that each one of them is absolutely indivisible. Then we will explain that if this statement is correct concerning motion or time, then it is necessary to judge conclusively that the body is composed of parts that cannot be further divided (al-ajzā' allatī lā tatajazzā'). And know that time, motion, and distance are three corresponding matters. If it is established for one of them that it is composed of indivisible things, then it is established for all three that they are likewise. And if it is established for one of them that it is divisible ad infinitum, then it is established for the others that they are likewise. As for the philosophers (al-falāsifa), they have established that the body is susceptible to infinite divisions. Then they have subordinated to that the notion that motion is divisible ad infinitum, and that time is divisible ad infinitum, and that it is impossible for time to be composed of instants (ānāt).
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the fourth chapter, where the author sets out his plan to prove the existence of the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) through arguments based on motion and time. He begins by stating two demonstrative proofs: first, that motion consists of discrete, indivisible occurrences in adjacent spaces; second, that time consists of discrete, indivisible instants. He then asserts that if either motion or time is composed of indivisible units, then it necessarily follows that the body itself is composed of indivisible parts (the atom). The author next introduces a principle of correspondence among three things: time (zamān), motion (ḥaraka), and distance (masāfa). If any one of them is composed of indivisible parts, the others must also be; conversely, if one is infinitely divisible, the others are as well. Finally, he contrasts his position with that of the philosophers (falāsifa), who hold that the body is infinitely divisible, and from this they deduce that motion and time are also infinitely divisible, denying that time could be composed of instants. Thus the author sets up the opposing view that will be refuted in the ensuing discussion.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
الحركة
الزمان
آنات
أجزاء لا تتجزأ
المسافة
الفلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument is circular: proving the atom from motion/time and then proving motion/time from the atom.
Correction: The author is not circular; he is stating a logical entailment: if motion/time are indivisible, then the body must be indivisible. He will prove the indivisibility of motion and time directly, then deduce the atom.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what are the three corresponding matters that must share the property of divisibility or indivisibility?
A) time, motion, and distance
B) time, space, and body
C) motion, rest, and distance
D) instants, atoms, and bodies

Key Takeaway
The author's strategy is to prove the indivisibility of motion and time, and then conclude that the body must be composed of indivisible atoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your body is composed of indivisible atoms that are constantly being recreated by Allah in each instant, how does that change your awareness of your dependence on Him for every moment of your existence? Will you remember this the next time you feel self-sufficient?
?
The philosophers claim matter is infinitely divisible, implying an eternal universe without a Creator. The author argues for atoms, which point to a Creator who brings things into existence. Which view makes you more accountable for your actions before Allah? Does your daily life reflect the belief that you are sustained atom by atom?
?
Consider the correspondence among motion, time, and distance. If any one of them is proven indivisible, the others follow. How does this interconnectedness of creation point to the oneness and wisdom of the Creator? Are you living as if your every motion and every moment is under His control?
